RATING: 7
Provisional rating based on 1 play.
The game is simpler than it looks to learn and play. But more complex than it looks to play well!
It is also a lot more vicious than it appears.

I love the mechanics in this game and they fit together really well.

The theme also works well - it is really cool to see that all of you face the same challenges/opportunities (effectively playing against the game) but then there are not enough solutions (in a 5 player game) for everyone to get through unscathed.

RATING: 8
My favourite game from 2007. It's very much a eurogame but at the gamers end of the scale and more interaction than most eurogames. Several strategies to adopt and all plays out in 90 mins. But the game system punishes you and recognising that you are going to take some hits and planning for it is key.

My highest rated game based on BGA plays alone. Fascinating decisions, seems to have good depth yet easy to learn and flows really nicely. I don't understand the "too harsh" view on this game that some have - the player who weathers the storms the best might come dead last! The disasters are constraints within which you have to work to get the most VP, and chances are if you're not losing people you're falling behind the 'greedier' players. 

Second Feld game I've played, second 9 I've given him - will have to try more of his work...

RATING: 8
Crisis management with a victory point engine humming along.

Seems to scale well, definitely a gamer's game, not sure if fun is a word I would use, but interesting to play.  Some variability each game with the order of events for the game, and the order of actions each round.  Need to always manage your money, resources, workers, homes, and point collecting.  

One complaint is the terrible components-  tiny, junk quality cards (!!!), and surprisingly flimsy action tiles.  A bit surprising after the solid components in Notre Dame (by the same designer, company).  It's also worth noting that while Notre Dame is definitely a different game, there is a slightly similar flavor here, although in Notre Dame it's more "fun" to be constantly staving off disaster.

Own the [boardgame=165348]Great Wall & Events[/boardgame] mini-expansion from the [boardgame=40688]Treasure Chest[/boardgame].

RATING: 7
A fairly simple, and logical, exercise in action-selection with a harsh killing-off phase whereby you lose much of your tableau each round. It's dry, but clever. Not Stefan Feld's best (I favour Luna and Castles of Burgundy), but still head and shoulders above many other designers' works.

Traded away for Keythedral. I have found after playing Luna, that each of Feld's games have felt rather dry in comparison. ITYOTD was just too lacking in personality. I wouldn't pick it over Notre Dame or Castles of Burgundy, which I also own, so decided it could find a better home.

RATING: 9
Otro gran Feld. Más sencillo y rápido que otros suyos, pero que hay que pensar y planificarse como si no hubiera mañana! (A veces no la hay xDD). No perdona fallos y es muy árido. El diseño es sobrio y la sensación del juego es fría incluso más de lo habitual en un euro, pero es un muy buen juego con una mecánica sencilla pero curiosa. Lo peor: El factor azar de barajar las losetas de acciones y ordenarlas en grupos, le da chispa al juego pero también lo puede hacer desmerecer mucho, siendo determinante si 2 acciones que buscaban los jugadores, da la casualidad que salen juntas en el mismo grupo... Dejando este detalle azaroso a parte, es un juego excelente.

RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

This is the first Feld game that has really fallen flat for me.  For one, the board is extremely drab and not much to look at.  Additionally, the person cards you play in order to gain a new person have no indication on the card of what the related tile does, and it's very easy for a player to discard the wrong card when choosing a person.

As far as gameplay goes, everything is pretty straightforward and there does seem to be deep strategic choices, but for a reason I can't put my finger on those choices didn't seem all that interesting.  The game is quite punishing in that there are lots of mechanics that cause players to lose what they have built up, so maybe that has negatively impacted my impression of the game.

I wouldn't mind giving this one another try before I finalize my feelings about it, but after the first play, it definitely falls far short of other Feld games I enjoy much more: Castles of Burgundy, Bora Bora, and Der Speicherstadt.

RATING: N/A
Abstract:
A game about preparing for the "worst" by Stefan Feld. Players have to prepare for different - mostly bad - events in the upcoming 12 months (rounds). Each round, actions cards are randomly distributed into sets depending on the number of players. By turn order, a player selects one set and pays a penalty if the set has already been picked. The player then executes the action of the chosen set. After that, players hire persons who improves the effect of one of the available actions.

Round overview:
a) Select a set of actions and then an action from that set
b) Hire a new person (maybe discard an existing person if there is not enough space in the palaces)
c) Event
d) End round scoring

Action overview:
a) Taxes: collect Yuan (money)
b) Fireworks: collect fireworks
c) Research: collect books
d) Harvest: collect rice
e) Buy privilege (provide victory points every round)
f) Build: create new or expand existing palaces - palaces provide victory points and they provide room to "store" additional persons (a palace must be occupied to prevent decay).
g) Military parade: move up on the turn order ("person") track
h) Pass: restock to up to 3 Yuan
Note: the strength of these actions depends on the persons each player has in his palaces.

Event overview:
a) Drought (discard rice or loose persons)
b) Mongol Invasion (VP for warriors or loose a person for weakest military)
c) Tribute (paying coins or loose persons)
d) Plague (provide medicine or loose people)
e) Fireworks (get VP for most firework tokens)

Pros:
+ Managing palaces and persons is dynamic: provide room for persons and hire/fire them when necessary
+ Not all negative effects can be avoided (finding best trade-offs)
+ Player order is very important, but can be mitigated by paying money
+ Competition for player order depends on which persons the players pick
+ Selecting a set of card requires interesting decisions: pick what one needs most or pick what other players need most and try to block them
+ Preparing ahead for the particular events is very challenging
+ Finding a trade-off between picking old or young persons (action efficiency VS climbing up the player order)
+ May provide a tense feel of survival
+ Minimalistic design
+ Wall mini expansion: adds a semi-coop element to the game but it sucks up a lot of actions which could be used otherwise, therefore, it should be played with higher player counts to distribute the additional wall action on all players
+ Super event mini expansion: adds some variety without adding complexity. Each game, the 7th event is accompanied by one randomly pre-chosen super event. So, players have to plan/prepare more ahead for that event
+ Plays well with 3-5 players. Two players works but feels differently because the first player can focus more easily on blocking the other player during the set selection phase.

Neutrals:
# Artwork is okay, functional, boring main board
# No luck involved
# All events are laid out in random order at the beginning of each game
# No hidden information
# All game elements appear in each game (no difference each game, except for the super event mini expansion)
# Strategical game: plan ahead for the next events
# Indirect player interaction: player order, selecting a set of actions, limited number of person tiles available
# Punishing if one is planing badly (no catch-up mechanism)
# More about efficiently surviving than creating something permanently
# Scoring happens between rounds and at the end of the game

Cons:
- Missing player aid: the game could be very easy to teach but an overview is missing to explain the relationships between the different persons, actions, and events
- Components have not been upgrade in the 10th anniversary edition, but it at least contains the mini expansions
- Set-up: a lot of person tiles need to be sorted (number of tiles depends on player count)

Thoughts:
What I like most is the minimalistic, elegant design: only a few intertwined mechanics create a lot of depth. I like the tension that the events create: sometimes you just want to survive the game somehow. It can be very harsh and punishing, you need a group which like this kind of game.

Similar games: 
* Peloponnes (also preparing for catastrophes)

Player: 2-5 | Time: 75-100m

RATING: 8
Players are Chinese rulers who spend their time trying to avoid the effects of the monthly events (mainly disasters) and make a profit from the few months when nice things happen. All players do this by building palaces, employing staff and obtaining resources to negate the effects of monthly events over a year(hence name of game). An interesting game where the players are fighting the game instead of each other although of course what your opponents do reduces your ability to do things. Thoroughly enjoyed all games played.

RATING: 7.5
I bought In the Year of the Dragon as a blind buy because it was on my recommended list. I needed an extra game to make it to free shipping and the theme and mechanics sounded appealing.

Lucky for me this game offers so much more than an appealing theme and mechanics. Everything in the game feels so tight and fluid. It has nearly seamless game play that rewards strategic thinking. The game is very punishing so I would not recommend it for those casual gamers who are just looking to play a light game.

The feel of the game can be very negative at times. You are walking up a seemingly endless hill for most of the game trying not to trip and break your leg in the process. Overall, this game gets better the more we play it. Definitely going to be a favorite for years to come.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
2-5(4,5)

I rated this poorly after playing the computer version. That was a mistake.

I get the impression that there are a lot here on BGG for which this is the only enjoyable Feld. Well, I'm of the other camp that thinks this is the weakest Feld I've yet played.

I still enjoyed it.

There are two games here: the obvious "survive the storm" game and then the much more nuanced "get points while doing it" game.

What's great is that, while related, these two layers are distinct and only eventually do you realize that it's this second game that matters. And then you have a bunch of really interesting decisions to make. Now you're not solely thinking about surviving the events (which, if that's your focus, can be pretty easy) but about how to maximize your points. Often, this means you want to survive most events but occasionally you'll want to let some folks go.

With few players it does get pretty easy to manage the crises, which isn't ideal, but I can imagine it gets a LOT tighter with more and I'm eager to play it as such.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At heart, this is yet another Euro-efficiency game, like the designer's previous effort, Notre Dame, which I didn't like at all.  So why do I like In The Year of the Dragon?  

Like Notre Dame, ITYD has damage limitation, yet implements it in a much more varied, painful and interesting manner.  There's a really tough balancing act between building a v.p. engine and staving off calamities.

The turn order mechanism is particularly well implemented and I've twice ignored it - to my cost.  Turn order is hugely important as the first choice of an action is free, but latecomers have to pay and money is really tight.  ITYD introduces another trade-off, between hiring veterans, with strong abilities but contributing few points towards turn order, and novices who offer the opposite. 

As with many good Euros, your actions are very restricted and there is always more you want to do - and do it before your opponents. You get just 12 actions plus the chance to hire 13 people. So every decision is significant, there is no fat on this game at all.  

Crucially for me, ITYD has a bit more interaction than many other euros, with players competing on many levels and their actions having clear effects on others.

I haven't yet played enough to comment on viable strategies, but there seems to be enough variability to prevent this game being solved and to make it (a bit) different every time.   

While others have grumbled about a pasted on theme, I think it is quite well applied and evocative.  The board is functional if not that interesting, the other artwork is nicely done.

RATING: 8
Very brutal game.  The key strategic insights to playing well is to: 1. recognize the importance of initiative, 2. know that you can't save everything, 3. plan ahead.  Most Euros I enjoy tend to have a balance between strategic and tactical play and that was my impression at first.  But at least with the opponents I go against, it turns out there is a strategy that is virtually full proof.  By going with the strategy of seizing and keeping initiative early in the game, I have only lost once, and that was because I deliberately took it easy with some new players and failed to realize that they were in better shape than I thought.  I think the only way this strategy fails is if an opponent consciously attempts to take back initiative.  The problem is that doing so will usually lose the game for both of us, so if I can creditably commit to the strategy first, it becomes suicide for the next guy....

RATING: 9.5
2 − 5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In the Year of the Dragon is designed in the same "disaster management" mold as ND except it pushes the envelop even more. Over a 12 turn game, there are in essence 4 peaceful turns. All other turns have a significant disaster that players have to deal with whether it's wealth generation for taxes, or food generation for droughts, or militia development to fend off the Mongols, etc... Probably the most significant difference between ND and In the Year of the Dragon is that all the events and the event order are known at the beginning of the game for In the Year of the Dragon. As such, there's a greater emphasis on long term strategy for In the Year of the Dragon. That being said, there are still some tactical elements as players struggle to gain the upper hand on the personality track, which determines play and action drafting order.

In the Year of the Dragon is definitely heavier than ND even though it plays in roughly the same time frame (roughly 1 hr). I think ND plays better at the lower end of the scale (2-3 players) while In the Year of the Dragon plays better at the higher scale (4-5). I suspect people who don't like the disaster management aspect of Notre Dame wouldn't like In the Year of the Dragon as it's even more brutal. People who enjoy ND will likely enjoy In the Year of the Dragon. Some "gamer" gamers will prefer the greater control afforded by the pre-ordained events.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I enjoy the planning aspect for overcoming events and scoring points!  However, if you choose the wrong starting characters, it's usually too difficult to get back into the game.  In the Year of the Dragon does feature a run-away leader problem if players fall behind in turn order or fail to see the importance of an early double privilege.  Being last in turn order is only a viable strategy if there is fierce competition for starting player amongst your competitors.  Perhaps your chances also depend on the order of events.  Although, the random grouping of actions provides a luck aspect, it also keeps the game from being solved.
 
The main downside to Year of the Dragon is the selection of starting characters.  I would prefer an auction format, but there is no official variant.  The other downside is that it can feel sort of scripted.  


[b]10 Jahre alea - Schatzkiste[/b]
[b]Great Wall of China expansion[/b] 

I made a homemade version based on the BGG files.  This expansion received mixed reviews.   


[b]10 Jahre alea - Schatzkiste[/b]
[b]Super Events expansion[/b] 

I made a homemade version based on the BGG files.  This expansion adds a bit more variety to the base game and only adds 1-5 minutes to the game length.   Having (1 out of 10) Super Events each game should keep things fresh and interesting.  If given a choice, I will always play with a Super Event.


[b]Solitaire variant[/b]
Maximum number of points in a game:
http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/320523

I created this unofficial variant in order to figure out the maximum number of points possible in a game and also to determine which actions/people were the biggest potential point generators.  It has been interesting comparing an early Double Privilege to a heavy Research strategy.  Perhaps Alan Kwan's double builder can outscore a Double Privilege?  Anyway, I have found that my scores in five player games have improved after attempting this variant.

RATING: 8
The decision-making in this game is brutal.  In a fun way.  A good-humored group will enjoy laughing at each other and themselves as they try not to shoot themselves in the foot (repeatedly).  I imagine this game will get more cutthroat as people tune in to the stronger long-term strategies, but even then, it's as much a game of recovering from mistakes (which will inevitably be made) as it is a game to win.  Unusual Euro-game in that it is very unforgiving and sometimes bleak, but very fun to play, as well.  In many ways, I enjoy playing this one just for the fun of tactically surviving the disasters, rather than for the actual win.

RATING: 8
Played this twice last night with 4 players. It was my seventh Feld game and so far I have pretty much loved all of them. Infact, I would have a very tough time ranking them individually - I clearly need to play them more!

Not only do I really enjoy Feld's games, I also seem to be quite good at them in general. I won both games last night. I employed somewhat similar strategies, but the two games still felt very different due to the randomness in setup which is a huge strength in almost every Stefan Feld game.

I also really appreciated the punishing feel of this. I like that you constantly have to avoid disaster. It makes for a more tense gaming experience than Felds more forgiving point salads where you just score points whatever you do.

So this game is definitely one I want to play more.

BS j
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The game design is outstanding. It's deeply strategic, richly tactical, and has deceptively simple mechanics. Every player is on equal footing with the same hiring capabilities and perfect knowledge of how the events will unfold each turn. So throughout the course of the game there is almost zero luck at all. Your strategy must factor in the choices of your opponents at every turn, requiring you to shift tactically. To keep your strategy moving forward, maintaining a strong position on the turn order track and/or enough coins is essential. It's an unforgiving game where you must be prepared to lose what you have built up. It's tough, it's painful, and it is utterly FANTASTIC! Every turn you do 2 simple things:  choose an action and hire a person. And that's it. But it's amazingly deep and every single choice is agonizing.

RATING: 7.25
Not one you'll get to grips with straight away, as you have a number of tiles to collect which have to be deployed to defeat a number of disasters that befall everyone, one at the end of each round. There's a "worker placement" aspect as the there is a role selection, boosted by any tiles you have, to get you food, healers, buildings, warriors, money, VPs or advances on the turn order track. You have to balance all that to get to win.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
An interesting game of disaster management.  So far I've only played the game with 2 players.  I imagine with 4 or 5 the level of difficulty will increase tremendously.

While there is some resource management, the real meat of the game is your ability to plan several turns in the future.  While the order of trials is known to all, players constantly have to adjust as the actions and personnel available change.  Plan well and you'll do all right.  Especially if you're making moves that will benefit you 2-3 turns in the future.  Fall behind or barely tread water and the game will drain you of your resources faster which results in severe penalties.

Overall the game is very euro however it doesn't feel stale or overly scripted.  The rules give you enough latitude to determine your own fate.  Also the scoring rules don't feel like they artificially keep the game close.  Good planners reap the benefits, bad planners suffer the consequences.

I rate the game at a 6.5.  Interesting challenges, nice theme, not much interaction and a little bit of fun.  I would like to play it again especially with 4-5 players.

*edit*  With more plays I've upped my opinion of the game.  There are more paths to victory than I first believed and its one of Feld's better designs.  Also 4p and 5p are best.

RATING: 7
Feld's second contribution to the Big Box series is a worthy addition IMO. It shares some qualities with the previous Notre Dame, but playwise executes much differently. Playing this feels somewhat akin to Citadels, not that it uses role-selection, but feels like a lesser form of that mechanic via the purchase of supporting personalities to maximize your gains or minimize your losses. The Asian theme is well-rendered and beautiful. Also like the addition of the "initiative track" to determine turn order. Another solid middleweight from Feld.

RATING: 9.5
It would rate a "9.5" if it wasn't for the double privilege being too powerful.

My favourite from Essen 07. It is decidedly a very good euro. Not enough resources to cover one's basis. You WILL suffer loss. A great planning game. This is a game of crisis anticipation & management!

This game can be very unforgiving. One misstep can trigger a cascade of total destruction. (I can personally attest to that one!) Brutal, engaging and addictive.

Very little luck (except for the division of the 7 actions) to ruin one's game, just decisions to minimize the misfortunes of disease, drought, the Mongols and the demands of the emperor.

Brilliantly replayable. The event order ensures this. Additionally, strategy changes depending on the event order and the actions & strategies of the other players.

Having played 3, 4 and 5 player games, Im Jahr des Drachen scaled well to all. And it is quite quick to play. (15 minutes per player)

Definitely Stefan Feld's best title to date (October 2007).

Update: I have increased the rating because it has been so well received by so many people at the game club that I play at. 

Second update: Decrease from 9 -> 8: though there are multiple paths to victory; the one major weakness in the design of the game is that a double privilege purchased in round #1 is very hard to beat. Despite trying multiple paths to victory, there is a heavy bias to the dominant strategy of buying a double privilege in round one. After 20 plays, it has lost its replayability because of this one issue.

Third Update: Have played with the Schatzkiste expansion (Great Wall and Super Event). Makes a tough game even more punishing. However, it doesn't fix the broken first-turn double privilege. It may have introduced a new problem. Playing the front constantly and building the wall seems to be a dominant strategy.

Fourth Update: We played with a variant wherein the first round, anyone may purchase the double privilege for 6 yuan. This seems to (mostly) fix the broken double privilege.

Fifth Update (June 2017): new official rules that a double privilege is $7. Seems to fix the one "broken" aspect to the original design over the past 10 years. Now bumped up to 9.5

RATING: 7
Just an update as I realize I've crossed the magic 10-play threshold on this one a while ago - I do like Year of the Dragon a lot, and it's by far my favorite of Stefan Feld's games. Clean-playing yet reasonably thematic, with lots of good tensions, this is definitely a winner.

---
Good fun, I enjoyed this more than I expected. I wasn't a huge fan of Notre  Dame, and in general I've felt like alea's big-box quality has been losing traction for a while. But this was very good, and not what I expected given the buzz. There is a fair amount of calamity, but it's not nearly the disaster management game the buzz has made it out to be. Sure, the repeated crises are what makes the game interesting, but you build up faster than you get cut down, so it's still a pretty rewarding building game. And it's definitely more substantive than some of the other highly-derivative, overwrought worker placement games we've been seeing of late (Cuba, Agricola); it manages to do a lot with a very clean game system, and have good depth without excessive play-time or pacing issues. In short, what we've come to love about alea. Time will tell, but the first impression is that this is a winner, and perhaps the most interesting and broadly appealing game in the line since Puerto Rico (I personally liked 5th Avenue better, but I realize that I'm on my own on that one). Of course, the line has been a bit soft since then.

RATING: 6
Rated "8" after one play. Very efficient game, where each turn you have 4 things you want to do... and one choice. It strikes me as a "worker placement" game, but you have only one worker. 

On the one hand, this will feel fairly familiar to anyone that plays plenty of Euro games, but what stands out after one play is the variety of ways to try and score, as well as one of the best approaches I've seen regarding turn order. Turn order is pretty critical. The game offers a separate turn order track that balances the ability to go first vs. gaining a premium on other rescources. Pretty neat game, and I can see myself picking this up at some point.

Update: Well, I picked it up, but it sat on the shelf for a while. I finally got in a 2nd play, and it's still solid. There's nothing terribly standout about the game, but I'm increasingly enjoying harsh systems or "games of survival," and ItYofD offers that in spades, along with the chance to do plenty of forward planning.

Update: a 3d play, and I'm liking this even a little more. This is a solid 8. Each game has felt a little different, and there's just a ton of game in a relatively manageable time frame and ruleset.

Update: well, my feelings on this one dropped and I ultimately traded it away. Two things bugged me. Regarding turn order, you're either sitting pretty in first, barely hanging on in 2nd, or totally hammered in anything later. Also, I just don't like the privileges. It's been the no-brainer choice in the groups I've played, and though there may be answers to it, it's simply not fun.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Im Jahr des Drachen is the newest alea game, so of course it had to be on my play list. Stefan Feld is apparently alea's in-house designer as of late, and I enjoy both of his previous games, Und Ru(h)m und Ehre and Notre Dame. I think Im Jahr is the best of his alea series to date though.

Im Jahr is a game of adversity. At the most baseline level, you're trying to build buildings, get people to put in them, and keep everyone alive. This is made difficult in various ways by the Cossacks (Mongols actually, but the swords look Cossack-like), the plague, droughts (with damage prevented by coarse-ground cocaine tacos), and the incredibly deadly king/emperor/ruler type of your choice (who will kill many more people than the Cossacks if you fail to grovel adequately).

Each round is based on two major halves. The first half, each player can select an action from a menu of X where X is equal to the number of players. Multiple people can select the same action, but they have to pony 3 bucks if someone else is already there. The actions each give you things like victory points, or chips that generate victory points, or chips which help you to avoid unpleasant events.

The second part of the game involves drafting personality tiles off the board. These tiles allow the actions you take in the first half of each round to be more efficient. They also give you points on the sub-track, which determines player order, and which tends to be very important if you aren't rolling in the cash.

After that, an event occurs, most of which are negative, and most of which involve losing people if you can't fend it off. The events are all laid out at the start of the game, so you can see what is coming, and everyone is likely to be dinged by something at some point in time. 

The end game scoring is the usual alea mish-mash with a tonne of things generating VPs, i.e.: the buildings you have put up, the number of people you have, points you've accrued throughout the game for having buildings, points you've accrued throughout the game for having bonus point scrolls, &c.

I think this game fits together well, and I enjoy the tough choices and the ability to look ahead at the events and craft a game length strategy. In some senses, people have been comparing it to Notre Dame in that it is a game where it is nice to have strong infrastructures in place so that one can take actions with increased efficiency and get a bigger return, but it's impossible to do everything well, and the order the events come out in seems like it will dictate some strategic choices.

My only real reservation about this game is my feeling that with a cash heavy infrastructure (i.e.: a couple of the coin-makers) you can almost ignore what the other players are doing. The only real interaction in the game is based on the battle for scarce actions, which is the major angst in the first half of the round, and also partially dictates person tile choice in the second. Cash allows you to effectively buy the action you want for 3, and to take the person tiles you want without paying much attention to the turn order sub-track. Not every game will make cash a sound choice, so this is a niggle, but so it goes. 

I like the game though, and I would like to play it some more to form a more solid opinion on it. This might have a hard ceiling on it where players can craft certain strategies for certain event sequences (i.e.: when this event occurs early do this...&c.) but that's complete speculation on my part. At this point, I enjoy, would be willing to play it anytime, but don't need to own it. 7 for starters.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
6/12/12:  Traded for Duel In the Dark + Duel of the Giants in No Cultists MT.

I'm sorry to see this game go.  I like it, but it doesn't interest my usual gaming friends so it tends not to hit the table much.  It isn't very good with two.  Hopefully the two Duel... games will be better.

Earlier:
When I first opened the box and readied the game for play I thought that the pieces and art were very nice.  A couple of turns in found me annoyed that the action cards, events, and people didn't reference each other more directly.  In other words, the art was enjoyable but did not help me to tie together which pieces helped with which pieces.  The game play was interesting.  I got a hard lesson early on to watch which people remain available when my opponent swiped all but one healer early in the game.  

With two people the game is okay.  I'm wondering if it plays better with more.  We didn't find the choices all that difficult to make nor did we work very hard to avoid losing people or palace floors.  It left me feeling like I'd missed key strategies.

ETA: the game is better with 5.  Competition made decisions tighter and more challenging.

ETA: better still with 3.

RATING: 6
This game is... how to put this... well, a dichotomy. On the one hand we have a game which is as much about creating an engine as it is about tearing it down in a controlled fashion. This is a very rare mechanism; I feel it is hardly ever used because gamers don't like to tear down things. They want to build, extend, engulf, etcetera. Nevertheless, IJdD flies in the face of conventional wisdom. And manages to pull it off quite well, certainly better than any other game I know. Which I admit isn't saying much due to an apparent lack of competition, but still if you are looking for this sort of thing then IJdD is your game.

Second plus is that IJdD is also very strongly about turn order manipulation. Stefan Feld uses the principle in almost all of his later games, but nowhere is the matter so explicit and central as in this game. It is horrendously expensive to go last all the time, and much of the game is weighing up the cost of being able to select first against the benefit of spending resources elsewhere to greater effort. The game is rather relentless here, which is unusual in its indirect interactive power. This is not a nice game where everyone can do whatever he pleases.

But... there is a downside, and that is that it turns out that you are played by the other players a bit too often in my opinion. Because resources are scarce in this game there will be times when you simply have no alternative, and certainly no creative alternative, to pick a certain action. It can be argued that minimising the consequences is precisely what IJdD is about, and I would agree. But that is not the point: the point is that the above simply cannot be avoided, and the result is the feeling of a straightjacket from which there is no escape until your opponents losen up the belts. Granted, you'll likely return the favour at some point, but still. I've played the title a handful of times now, and I still don't like how I'm being shackled thusly. It makes my participation feel pointless, and I dislike that intensely.

So all in all there are good reasons to like IJdD, but for me there is also good reason to dislike the game. Hence the dichotomy I alluded to earlier, and hence the ambiguous rate I've given the game. Still, the title is good in its own right and worthy of a trial run or two if you haven't yet played it. And if you worry about finding yet another point salad thingie: not here. This design goes back to the time when Feld was doing things rather differently. Apart from the turn order mechanism there is very little which would give away that Stefan is the author of IJdD.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
F'n Great!  A bit of luck, but not enough to skew the results by much, generally (and any "screw factor" can be negated by using certain strategies).  Diverse strategies that are still effective, loads of player interaction without being too easy to destroy opponent's strategies, lots of decisions, lots of tough decisions... what more could you want?

2011 Edit: I need to drop this guy's rating from where I originally had it.  High initiative mass-build is simply too good, and dominates the strategy.  The question is, do I give it a 7 based on the time it was interesting for, or a 5 for where I'd put it now?  I'll compromise and give it a 6.

De esta manera, teóricamente, se puede competir sobradamente contra la estrategia ganadora, teniendo al menos un turno para ello, a parte de los personajes iniciales.[/COLOR]

RATING: 5
I have a feeling this rating will rise after multiple plays, BUT here is why I rated the game so low, and why it probably will never go beyond a 6.5/7:

There are games on a learning curve, then there is Year of the Dragon.  All information is perfect information; there is no real luck factor in this game.To me, the only way this game could be enjoyable is if everyone was at the same skill level.  There is a lot of blocking and player "screwage", so the player that gets messed with the least could more than likely win most of the time.

The positive:  The tension and screwage part of the game.  (But it is a double edged sword).

For newbies:  I reccomend: http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/23763/page/7

Awesome tutorial on In the Year of the Dragon, to give you a taste of what I am talking about. (The game is 171 on this geeklist.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
You (the players) are Chinese rulers, and the next 12 months (turns) you will encounter droughts, Mongol attacks, sickness, and other events. Fortunately you know when these events will happen. So, hire the right personnel at the given moment, and let those farmers grow rice before the drought, have those soldiers protect your palace, and get docters to heal the sick, or suffer the consequences. Oh, yes. To house that personnel, you need carpenters to enlarge your palace.

Some workers are more efficient then others, but those let you loose initiative in the selection of the action that one of your workers will do that turn. 

Nicely themed and challenging game. Since I only played it once, I don't know about the replayability. But I look forward playing it again.

RATING: 7
The method of selecting actions, soft-locking others out of them (since they can be paid for after having been previously selected) and

The cut-throat nature makes it a very interactive game - there's always competition whether for picking your starting people, selecting actions or trying to have the most fireworks and not the least armies.

The rules seemed a bit fiddly - it took me a while to understand what was going on, finally realising that people do nothing by themselves - only with actions or events do they have benefit - and Ian using 'gold' to mean 'unit of money' when there are silver/gold pieces aided my confusion. Even after the game, I still felt a bit shaky about a couple of things.

I'm sure I'll enjoy it more after getting to grips with the rules.

There's definitely a LOT of potential for cutthroat interaction, despite it all being technically indirect and that's something that will surely give the game lasting power.

And even though the scoring track indicates someone way out in front, it's hard to tell where you stand in the game.

Nearly impossible to recover from a mistake and I thank God for the flash version as I can simply shut it down and restart.

RATING: 7
This is a good design and it probably deserves a higher rating, but the key differentiating factor of this game is the damage control, which I don't really enjoy planning for. RATING: 6.75
I've only played this game twice, but both times have been very enjoyable.  The game is very clean and has few exceptions.  So it is fun on your first game and also has enough depth to keep you interested in future games.  Even though the game favors clean mechanics over theme, it still does a good job of invoking a theme.   I'm a little worried that I will enjoy it for another 5 games, and then it will feel old.  It all depends on how much variation the set-up of the "end of round" tiles provides.  As for now, this is one of my favorite new games (1/27/2008).  It feels a little bit like Notre Dame (not surprisingly).  My only grip is that I can usually see who is going to win several rounds before the end of the game.  So I play with the inevitable feeling of doom approaching, and there is nothing I can do to change it (this being with the games where there is nothing I can do to catch up).  Some sort of hidden scoring would have solved this problem.  Then I would have had the sense of an inevitable doom approaching mixed with a tinge of hope.

RATING: 7
After 1 play, I'm starting this out at a 7.  It's gives you some interesting choices.  Half of the game is selecting a role ala Puerto Rico, but only you get to take the action.  The other half is recruiting a "person".  The "person" gives you bonus resources when its corresponding action is taken.  Those resources, in general, generate your VPs.  On the negative, I couldn't really explain the theme to you or what it all really means.  Recruit some dudes into your buildings, avoid losing dudes to disasters, use those dudes to generate VPs (and make space for more dudes).  So the "7" I give it now is because it is new, and I want to play it again.  After a few more plays, I expect the itch to be sufficiently scratched...and the game drop to a 6.

RATING: 8
Players represent Chinese nobles, claiming prestige and avoiding bad events.  Players take turns drafting actions from a "slice the pie" selection.  Then they get to recruit a person into their palace, who confer abilities to help them deal with events.  Finally, the game turn event triggers which usually means bad things if the players are not prepared.  Prestige (victory) points are awarded for palaces, and a few other things at the end of each turn.  Most points wins.  Interesting, if rather derivative game, that feels rather brutal if players don't plan carefully.  Feels two steps forward, one step back at times.  I'm starting the think the "action selection" mechanic is getter a bit over-wrought now, but as a game, there's nothing wrong with it.  Good game.

RATING: 9
An excellent euro that has all players scrambling to survive upcoming disasters. The scoring is always close and the game is a real struggle right to the end. Plays well with 4-5 players and has everyone working together to survive while stealing actions from each other to do so. Highly recommended, but not for the faint of heart. Some players will get completely devastated by this game. That said, I've won with many temples full of people and I've won with all but 3 people discarded due to one event or another. If teaching first time players, perhaps consider curating the events and explaining to them how to prepare for what is coming.

60-90m, best with 4-5 players. 10+ plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In the Year of the Dragon might be the most surprisingly oppressive game I've played. I did not expect this game to so easily, nor so often, back me into a corner, and I quickly realized that a strong early game is crucial. This game is all about assessing the events, and creating a careful plan of averting disaster. With only twelve actions at your disposal, one misstep or loss of focus could spell disaster. Though not a point-salad as is Feld's preference, this game instead relies on complex connections between various decisions; take an old worker, who is more proficient, but fall behind in player order; or take a points action, but forgo useful resources. It falls in on odd space of games that are easy to teach, but highly punishing for new players, which certainly detracts from its appeal.

RATING: 7

RATING: 6

RATING: 8
I had a demo at Essen and this was my first real play and I won, but i think this is a neat package. There are different ways to build up your score, money is very tight, getting ahead on the person track early in the game helps a lot, but running a balanced game makes a big difference. Using your jokers well is crucial, the whole thing works very nicely and feels different from other Alea games, or most heavier games. The player who started badly scored massively right at the end and came second by 1 point, and the player who'd been challenging me the whole game imploded on the last turns and limped home 20 points adrift. I think the the evnt order is very significant, and in our game, the money and hammer actions came out in the same group on the first four turns, crucial in limiting the cash flow in the game. I negelcted the end scoring, but just kept my nose ahead. I put a big emphasis on keeping 1st place on the person track and so getting first action, and happily sacrificed farmers, builders, tax officials and doctors when they had served their purpose. I'm happy to play this again, there's definitley something here and I enjoyed the choices.

RATING: 10
WOW!  What a *brutal* and challenging game this is!  A lot of preparation is needed to overcome the disasters and casualities that come your way.  Careful planning is essential to having success.  I believe this game is an improvement over Notre Dame (a good game in its own right).  There is minimal luck with several disastrous elements and strategic paths.  In terms of the game's depth vs. playtime, this title offers the best bang for the buck that I have seen thus far.

Players manage money, actions, palaces, people/helpers, initiative, and future events all while trying their best to figure out the most efficient method of grabbing victory points.  I disagree with the comments stating this has little interaction.  I find it has quite a lot of it.  While there is no way to "attack" someone, the interaction involves monitoring relative position on various aspects with plenty of opportunities to royally screw your opponents!

Mistakes are unforgiving - but please don't let this deter you from giving this game the chance it deserves.  Stay with it and I think you may find some very interesting dynamics at play here.  It's such a treat to experience an aggressive game with clever mechanics and a "painfully" rough system.  No doubt this is a "gamer's game" - and it's a delightful one!  May be the best games to be released this year, and is climbing its way to reach my personal Top 10.  Grade: A+

RATING: 6
It's a brain burner ladies and gentlemen. It's a game that's out to get you, and despite the smiling faces on the cover, this game doesn't like you very much at all. 

The theme can be basically be described as - "Life as a middling ruler in ancient China sucked, and you just got handed the job". 

Your little province is in bad shape, and unless you do some clever management and planning, things will get even worse. Famine, War, Disease, they're all there. Are you up to the task?

I don't know if I'm up to the job, and I can't decide if this game is a masterpiece or something that's best left for the masochists. I will play this again however, and see if it's really worth all the pain it puts you through. 

Eliminated in the great game purge of 05/30/2010.

RATING: 6
Played 1 time with 3 players. I feel bad giving this game only a 6, but it is the straw that broke the camel's back for me: I am getting sick of heavy Euros. This game is unbelievably tense, well balanced, and cleverly designed. But it has almost no connection to theme and is, well, too perfectly balanced. I have played about 400 Euros and it's like I am suddenly disenchanted with the heavier, abstractive Euros like Goa, In The Year of The Dragon, and Puerto Rico. Princes of Florence was once a 10 for me; later a 9, now a 7. It's like I can feel the systems and that perfectly tuned math too much. So strange to arrive at this opinion after enjoying these games for so long. No my game tastes run to middleweight shorter Euros (Manila, Attika) or hybrids (Agricola). No fault of this came, it's just so...done for me. The heavier Euros need more flavor. The bar has been raised and the German publishers have no really answered the challenge (except for Lokout Games!)

RATING: 7.5
Delighted with this game.  There are two tracks: a victory point track and a player order track which is unique and a good game mechanic.  Players have to decide whether they advance on the victory point track or the player order track.  If players are always last on the player order track it is likely they will lose the game because they were unable to get what they needed to win the game.  Players acquire people essential to helping them stay alive through a series of disasters.  It is almost impossible to keep everyone alive and so players are hit with the often hard decision of whom dies.  Keeping your people alive will automatically hose other players, especially if you are first on the player order track.  I had a hard time taking this one out of the box because the contents looked a little boring--I am so glad I finally played.  Not a boring game at all--and I do like the pieces.  People playing this game are at a great disadvantage againist a player who has played it once.

RATING: 5
Pretty interesting role-selection and worker-selection game where a lot of your time is spent butressing yourself against disasters.

Duration: The game takes about an hour and a half but it goes by fairly quickly. There isn't too much downtime.
Design: The variety comes from the order of the disasters, and you will play differently as a result.
Strategy: There are plenty of choices and risks to be taken, and a few different strategies that you can take.

Theme: This is a Eurogame at its heart but the 12 months and disasters were pretty interesting.

RATING: 9
Love this game! 

This is a game where you try to plan and prepare for the calamities ahead, fight for turn-order control (or adjust your strategy based on the actions of people who go before you), and try to remember to score VP while you're at it. The theme really draws you in. 

Agonizing decisions - everything is some sort of trade off, and you basically have to choose which losses you can handle, and which you can't. Lots of different ways to score/win, and lots of ways you could really get hurt. 

It plays out fairly quickly, with turns and phases going very fast. Because planning ahead is what this is all about, you have to be constantly engaged and the experience really satisfies.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After 22 games with AI (http://www.silvermask.co.uk/dragon2.swf)

This game is a bomb.

How Stefan Feld may have committed a game so tight, strategic, tactical, interactive, ruthless with players with so few components (a few tiles and character cards, money, 7 actions tiles, 12 events tiles) and rules so simple and pure ?

For me, only one response : this guy is a genius.

It would take full pear throughout the game, even if some events are less of a disadvantage than others:
- Day of the dragon if you have already established a good VP engine (court ladies, palaces, scholars, privileges)
- The Mongol invasion that can even turn to your advantage to discard a character become useless

The turn order's mechanism is a model of its kind, forcing players to choose between power and character position in turn order. The order of play is crucial in this game unless you have builded a good money engine. In fact, money is scarce and allows you to keep good latitude in your action selection.

Throughout the game, you are torn between:
- Ensuring the expansion of your palace (every turn a new character must be hosted, VPs at the end of the game) but not too much (you must feed them during the drought event (1 one rice by occupied palace))
- Ensuring a VP engine but it will prevent you from doing something else (make money, expand your palaces, producing rice, fighting for turn order...)
- Choosing the effective character to the detriment of turn order
- Deliberately suffer some events and sacrifice characters to the detriment of actions power and VPs at the end of the game

A game of planning, anticipation, adaptation to the choices of other players (eg prevent another player to win 11 VP per turn if he has 4 scholars in his palace), hand management, where you must find a balance.

Unclassifiable game whose appeal and addiction come, IMHO, from frustration and violence imposed to the players.

Simply awesome.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The year of the dragon is a rather desolate year. "A long string of disasters" would be a more appropriate description.

Fortunately, the disasters that will strike are all known well in advance so it is possible to prepare for them. In theory. Unfortunately, there is never enough time, nor money, nor people to hire to avert everything that comes flying at you. The result is not pretty. Your palaces will crumble. Someone will get hurt. And die.

And all of that is a lot of fun. There are various approaches to disaster avoidance/recovery, and very different ways to score points, yet they are well-balanced and can lead to very close results. In addition to the race for VP there is the equally important jockeying for position throughout the game since the early birds can get actions for free while the late-comers have to pay for them. Hiring weaker workers is usually advantageous here, but obviously they don't perform that great. Overall, lots of stuff to think about, and lots of ways to go about it.

Just don't get emotionally attached to the people you hire.

RATING: 9
Ein Jahr der Mühsal. Der Meister des Mangels. Mit keinem Spiel von Stefan Feld vor oder danach hat Stefan Feld sich selbst derart auf den Punkt gebracht. Im Gesamtkunstwerk des eurostilbestimmenden Autors bleibt das Frühwerk JAHR DES DRACHEN die komprimierte Meditation seiner eigenen typischen Spieleigenschaften, in denen es meist in mehr oder weniger vorder- bis hintergründiger Form um die Bewältigung von Schwierigkeiten geht, die sich wie ein Stachel ins Fleisch des Spielerlebnisses bohren. Dies ist womöglich der größte Beurteilungs-Irrtum, dem die Spiele von Feld unterliegen, denn gerade dieser Stachel ist das Zen(trum) der Feldschen Kreationen: viele empfinden den Mangelschmerz in seinen Spiele als nervig, hinderlich, dämpfend und messen die Spiele lieber am "Siegpunktsalat", den Feld oberflächlich oft auftischt. Dabei ist es gerade der offene oder verdeckte Stachel, der das grundsätzliche Spielerlebnis bei Feld ausmacht, weil der Mangel eine Wirklichkeit widerspiegelt, der sich die meisten Menschen ja doch ausgesetzt sehen: Probleme im Leben. JAHR DES DRACHEN kon(zen)triert sich in seinem dazu passenden minimalistischem, reduzierten Auftreten exakt auf diese Mißstandsrealität und kommt dabei ungeschminkt daher wie ein östliches Zen-Kloster. Wem es gelingt, sich am wenigsten zu beklagen und mit Lebensschmerzen wie Krankheit, Hunger, Überfall und Ausbeutung am besten zurechtzukommen, der darf sich als Sieger fühlen. Das kann auch der Letztplatzierte einer Partie sein. JAHR DES DRACHEN war jahrelang nur überteuert zu bekommen, die Jubiläumsausgabe verschafft jedem wieder einen günstigen Zugang zur Übung in Demut und Bescheidenheit. Geduld und das Warten auf bessere Zeiten wurde vom Leben schon immer belohnt.

RATING: 8
Wow! What a great Feld game! I'm a little late to the party trying out YotD, but of course I'm glad to have finally gotten this one out onto the table. The multiple events and scoring opportunities reads a bit complicated, but it actually plays very well. I really enjoy how all the different parts come together. It's one of those games that has a lot of little things going on but it comes together so seemlessly that there is no need to look up the rules again once the game is underway. Having to decide whether to benefit in one area while being hampered in another is nothing new, but Feld makes it feel fresh. It comes off feeling a bit repetitive by the end and benefits from players taking their turns quickly. There is development in the game but not enough to make all 12 rounds feel highly engaging at all times.

RATING: 8.8
Good grief, what a top game.  The ten events whose order changes from play to play mean it'll be hard to get tired of this, and actions are hard enough to come by that it feels a lot like Agricola--you know that there are several things that you ought to be doing, but there just isn't enough time.  You'll have to let something slide, and hope that the other players have an even tougher time of it.  Do not play this game if you feel the need to be a moral gamer, though.  To win, you have to ruthlessly calculate exactly how long a person deserves space in your court before you jettison them.  Imagine hiring a healer to help you hold onto several of your people, then allowing the next contagion to rid you of--that very healer.  Ah well, you needed room for the warrior who you'll sacrifice next turn, right after he helps you against the Mongol horde.  This game is just too, too much fun.  And gorgeous, too.

RATING: 7
It's hard not to see a lot of Notre Dame here.  Notre Dame was mediocre at best for me, a dull optimization game that played much the same every time.  But In the Year of the Dragon is much more sophisticated, more demanding, more interactive, tighter, and plays differently almost every time thanks to the variable setup.  A friend who has watched me win several times said "I keep watching to see what you do to win, but it's completely different every time."  I think that says a lot for the game.  There are many well-balanced ways to win, and you have to adjust your strategy based on the order of the events and based on what the other players are doing.  I like the   way the game presents you with too many challenges, forcing you to pick your poisons.  I like the Chinese court setting, which is evoked well.  I like that the game plays quickly.  I like that it's very easy to explain to new players.  Good game.

RATING: 7
An enjoyable game, if you don't mind constant harrassment from various catastrophes throughout the "year."  There are lots of very interesting decisions to make, most especially in selecting the older (more effective at what they do) or younger (less effective, but are more likely to help you be the first to take your turn the next round) helpers.  There are many elements to help you to victory in this game, and it's up to you what you want to focus on: turn order (who goes first is very important), big money, earning VP during the game at the expense of the current month's event, building several palaces to hold lots of helpers, or focusing on end game scoring.  

After the twelve event tiles are laid out, the game is fairly deterministic much like Agricola, although here you are selecting actions instead of placing workers.  The player interaction is much like Agricola as well ("You completely just took my space")

RATING: 7
I was really impressed by this game.  The interaction is nearly nonexistent, and it is multiplayer solitaire, BUT with multiple possible strategies to win, and with the variable turn mechanism adds replayability to a game that otherwise would have none.  The components look good except the butt-ugly and boring board.  There are a bunch of different actions, people, and events in the game that keep things interesting.  The fact that there is really no interaction takes a lot of the shine off.  I don't think that the game would stand up to repeated plays, even with the variable events to keep things spicy.  The only way any of the players can effect the others is through taking an action before them so they have to pay to take it.  I think the game will be "solved" soon, with the single best strategy coming to light, and no one will ever play this again.  There just isn't enough interaction here to warrant repeated plays.  HOWEVER, taking into consideration the lack of interaction, I really like the game for what it is.  If you are the type who wants a short multiplayer solitaire game where you build your little empire and sort of watch as others try to do the same, I'm not sure you could do any better than this game.  Certainly not in 45 minutes!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Even after the first play, I definitely liked it.  With 4 players, the game is competitive, but not too tight, so it's a good intro for newbies.  With 5 players, I hear the game is tight on resources.

I like the balance of needing medicine, rice, gold, and helmets to avoid losing guys.  I like the tradeoffs of choosing high initiative vs. getting more powerful workers (e.g. young monk gives you 6 initiative, but double rice and double medicine give you only 1 or 2 initiative but are very effective at helping you from losing workers).  In the first game I played, I was last on the initiative track by a large margin, and never produced gold (except for getting the charity 3 several times for being poor), but still came in a strong second, only 5 points back, because I had 5 houses, kept 8 guys alive, and got 12 points from monks.  The key, like in games like Puerto Rico or Caylus, is to choose a different path than other players, so that you will have less conflict and get actions for free and workers before they run out.  This will definitely be hard in a 5-player game, which I'm looking forward to playing.

I've played it many times as a 2-player game -- it can be done within 40 minutes pretty easily, and 30 minutes if you rush.  It's a fun, balanced game for 2.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Extremely tight game where every decision counts as players try to advance in VP's while preparing for various coming disasters. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
[Initial Impression: 4]
This game is almost entirely about forward planning - almost too much so. By the last 4 to 6 turns, the course of the game is likely to be decided and the remaining choices will be fairly obvious. It is important to look at the layout of the event tiles, plan a course for the game, and carry out that plan. Even a minor error (which can be an error of failing to select the point-optimizing option for yourself or one of failing to select an action that another player desperately needs and would not be able to pay for) can put a player well out of the running, especially if other players effectively capitalize on such errors. Turn order is incredibly important, especially early on, and giving up a point or two in favor of a better turn position can provide large returns. The game is very front-end loaded in that the build and privilege actions and the Court Lady all become worth less and less over time, but you do need to know when the proper time to take the Court Lady will be, as this selection will almost certainly put you behind in turn order. 

I believe the constraints on choices, the predetermined course of the game, and the straight-forward trade-offs among point payouts make this game "solvable" in the sense that it can be played in an algorithmic fashion (if X, then do Y, else Z, etc. - such an algorithm would have to adapt to the layout of event tiles and, to some extent, to the actions of other players). This makes for a mostly boring game that feels highly deterministic. The constant shuttling between players, while reducing downtime in face to face plays, makes the game drag during turn-based online play. The feeling of tension that many seem to enjoy feels artificially induced to me, and vanishes once you realize that your people are expendable under the right circumstances and that it is better to give them up if doing so will let you take an action that will net you more points over the course of the game. I make these comments from the perspective of someone who won our 5-player game by a substantial margin, but nonetheless felt the last third of the game essentially played itself out. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game has so much going on that it's hard to know where to begin- players need to excel in military- and food; they need medicine, and money; they need to collect some direct VP's, and perhaps some fireworks too.

Stefan Feld has certainly created a roller-coaster of a year in a Chinese satrap, and as he has progressed he has gotten more clever in how the themes interweave with his always clever game mechanics. (Oh yes, and his penalties are also "more thematic".)

To me this one comes across as just a little too frenetic midgame (where one is either cresting the tide, or falling deep enough under the wave that they're not coming back up again), and then perhaps a little too predictable at the end, where only one or two calamities are left, and defenses against the completed problems fall by the wayside, for mass point accumulation to the best of one's abilities.

I've played this a couple times, and while I'd call it a good game, I disagree with those thinking it Feld's best (could be Trajan, or Burgundy, or...).  

I've given this a 7 to start with, and until it impresses me as better than that it's staying there.  A nice effort, but not my favorite Feld.

RATING: 6
A "legszemettebb" "legszívatósabb" Feld játék. A játékosok 9 segítő közül választhatnak. Az építőmesterrel palotát építhetünk, ahol segítőink laknak, az adószedővel pénzhez juthatunk, a tudóssal ponthoz, a katona harcban segít, a gyógyító a fertőzés ellen véd, a szerzetes a játék végén ad pontot, a hölgy a játék közben,a tűzijátékos a tűzijáték rendezésben segít (szintén pontokat hoz), a földműves az etetésben segít. 

A segítőkhöz kapcsolódó 7 akció közül választhatunk, amelyek véletlenszerűen vannak felosztva annyi felé, ahány játékos játszik. A segítők sorszáma alapján választunk akciót. Aki hátrébb áll nagyon lemarad, mert választhatja ugyanazt az akció csoportot is, amelyet a korábbi játékos, de ezért 3 yüant kell fizetni, márpedig ez rengeteg pénz ebben a játékban. Tehát aki lemarad a segítők sorszámában, az jó eséllyel kikap. 

Ráadásul minden segítségre szükségünk van, mert minden hónapban jön egy negatív esemény (császári pénzbeszedő, aszály, mongol támadás stb). Amelyiknél szinte mindig veszítünk segítőket (5-ből 4 esetben). 

Az embernek az az érzése, hogy az győz akinek sikerül úgy játszania, hogy minél kevesebbet veszítsen. A játék során végig ez az érzés munkálkodik az emberben.  Ezzel a játékkal biztos nem fogja megszeretni senki sem a Feld játékokat,sem a társasjátékokat. Gondolkodós játék, de csak a frusztrációt jól tűrők részére.

RATING: 8.6
Dec17:

Almost a decade later I came back to this in it's "10th anniversary form".  I actually bought the 10th anniversary of this and Notre Dame (this mostly as an add-on).

And ya know, I appreciate this game a lot more now.  Perhaps I just am better ignoring the "passive" and focusing just on the "aggressive" portion of this game.

I've also grown to appreciate the cohesiveness of the game.  The interaction between people, actions and events is just awesome.

And the non-cyclic turn order is annoying, but I've also grown to appreciate the benefits more (and just gotten more aggressive about yelling at people to take their turn).

Still, it can totally knock a player out of the game, which isn't cool.



Original comment (circa 2008):

Sadly, not a great follow up to Notre Dame IMHO.  I still enjoy the multiple paths to victory and the reasonably easy to grasp game play.  Unfortunately, a lot of negatives were introduced:

1. The rats are back, but this time they can knock someone right out of the game.  One played missed buying an action by $1, and proceeded to lose 5 people over 2 turns.  They were finished, but still had 45 minutes of game left.

2. I hate player turn reordering.  It adds tons of time as each player has to of course ask "Who's turn is it?"

3. I've come to really despise "worker placement"/"action selection".  It's the most passive aggressive form of player interaction I've encountered.

RATING: 8
In the Year of the Dragon is a similar style of game to Stefan Feld's previous effort for Alea: Notre Dame. Both games feature a set of mechanics that are melded together successfully into a game that is relatively easy to learn and quick to play, with the outcome depending on the cumulative result of numerous tactical decisions. I prefer In the Year of the Dragon both because of the theme, which is a little more integrated into the game, and the higher level of tension. The only slight problem, it seems to me, is that early purchase of a Double Privilege is disproportionately powerful. If all players are aware of this the problem is minor, but if there are newbies in a game they are likely to be at a significant disadvantage.

RATING: 9
See my review after 20 plays for full commentary: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/274044/agri-what-best-gamers-game-come-out-awhile

Solid follow-up to Notre Dame (this is better actually) and an excellent addition to the Alea line up (read a Gamer's game). Things are very tight, tense and unforgiving again here and the bad shit is coming your way so start planning where to take the hit!  

While it has a nice theme it is only loosely  tied to the mechanics but I still enjoyed the theme being there at any rate. So many things you need to do here and not enough actions to do them, yep, great turn angst here on several levels. One of the main things you are doing is trying to minimize almost certain loss while building an efficient VP engine at the same time. You are also trying to recruit some good workers HOWEVER while the better worker provides more it also has a bigger price which is paid by moving less on the turn order track, angst again and nice balance as well. Turn order is huge in the game as actions are free for the first taker, sloppy seconds or more will cost you 3 Yuan so you really don't want to go this route but alas you just might have to at times. This is where someone can make a bit of a breakaway however we have found that it is a LOSER breaking away (usually from a critical error) not a winner as the system is pretty unforgiving so runaway LEADER is NOT accurate, there is a difference.

Comparing Notre Dame, and they should be compared as they do scratch the same itch (building a VP machine while minimizing loss, card driven, etc), I much prefer having all my cards in hand from the beginning and being able to plan when to play them throughout the game versus the card drafting of ND. The other big difference from ND is that the events are preset for each round right from the beginning so again planning is rewarded much more versus never knowing what abilities or bad stuff might be coming (ie mice) let along cards. While in ND you need to watch the mice here there are four different bad events that happen twice each, this means 8 of 12 rounds are bad but some a lot more so than others and you do know when they are coming so at least you have a chance to prepare for them. This also might be a little more interactive although it is close but here there is a very nice battle for turn order that really adds to the turn angst when hiring characters every turn, if your able to 'jump' on top of someone on the initiative track you get to go before them which can be quite important and affect your choices. Watching the other players might also be a little more important here as to how to possibly adapt your strategy 'around' theirs and if you are watching close enough screwage is possible. Now don't get me wrong, I quite like ND however if someone suggests both I would pick this almost every time as they are both similiar (they feel different but the core game is the same) only here there is less luck and more planning which I prefer.

Very little luck overall actually, lots of planning, and so far looks like multiple ways to win although a couple do look to be superior but it still seems quite balanced. Playable in about 60 minutes with 3 or roughly 15 to 20 minutes per player which is nice and quick and probably best with 3 or 4 players. More Euro goodness in Alea clothing.

[u]UPDATE:[/u]8 => 8.5 - 5 plays in and the game is becoming a very nice challenge as you fight to beat the game AND your opponents (like ND again here) and it scales very well 3 to 5. Still a little leary on the multiple strategies but I think at least 3 dominant ones will emerge and one thing is certain, being able to adapt your strategy on the fly can be a critical skill in this as is watching what others are doing and trying to work around them. This game has scored big points in our group for 'post game discussions' and actually had me calculating out strategies to the point I wrote them down which I have never done before. I have a strategy article pending which I will post soon. 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] 8.5 => 9 Fantastic TENSE, TIGHT and BRUTAL game, 7 plays in and it really feels like you are in a vice being squeezed throughout the game and something is going to pop...and pop you will! Crisis management in spades here; planning where, when and how bad to take the hits is key. This is the core game and it certainly won't appeal to everyone but our group of 'Gamer's' love it and love the challenge it presents each game!

Replay is there thanks to the order of events being different game to game, the order of how the actions are grouped and even having to adapt to what others are doing which all also ensure that just one strategy will not work. POF & TAJ fans will dig the game-long planning, multiple paths and tension among other things. 

Multiple paths have been confirmed along with scaling well 3 to 5 however I think I am now leaning towards the very tight 5 player game is best theory. The game is strangely addicting and FUN as you are always trying to beat the game and the other players AND maybe even yourself, that is trying to beat your prior score and trying to out-manage what you did last time. If I'm 20 games in and I still dig it this much it could hit a 10 for me, more games to confirm. 

Thank you Stefan, can't wait to see what you have in store after Roma II ...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Reminds me of Princes of Florence, in that I have to have a vague plan going in, and then stop about halfway through and figure out exactly what I need to do for the rest of the game. It's good stuff. Rating may end up rising to an eight.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
In the Year of the Dragon is a very tight game that requires careful planning and efficient play to minimize your losses, which will inevitably occur from at least one (and likely more) of the events plaguing 1000 A.D. China, i.e., drought, contagion, Mongol invasion, and bankrupting taxation.  Aside from shuffling and separating the action cards into groups for priority selection each round, there is no luck here, making this a very tense game where I find myself spending a lengthy amount of time evaluating my options.  There is a lot of fun and deep strategy here.  In a way, it reminds me of Bruges (same designer) stripped of its randomness and victory point free-for-all and injected with agonizing decisions and foreboding events that conjure up an intense struggle.  I've really been enjoying the game experience thus far.

The components and game art, however, are not very inspired (for my taste, at least).  While the aesthetics don't take away from the gameplay, this game really deserves some higher production values.

Weighted Rating: 8.1 (Fun 9; Gameplay 9; Components 6; Theme 6)
Formula:
[Weighted rating = (((Fun*4) + (Gameplay*3) + (Components *2) + (Theme *1))/10)]

2015-05-01

With even more plays, my appreciation for In the Year of the Dragon has grown a lot, now it is a solid 7.0 for me. Nowadays I prefer to play hard and punishing games and ITYOTD fits the bill perfectly. I still want to buy it but I have been unable to do so (I already have the mini expansions :D).

As with many of my favorite games, it has a steep learning curve, a positive for me. This also means an experienced player will crush a new one but this didn’t bother me when I was the crushed one. 

The art and game board are of regular quality.

Bottom line, it is a good game I usually want to play, it packs hard decisions (who lives and who dies) and high tension in a relatively short play time.
 
One of 2007´s favorites.

It's taken over from Arkadia as my favourite game from 2007... an easy '9.'

RATING: 6
Not my cup of tea.  I like the pervading nihilism that you're going to get destroyed by taxation, famine, war, or disease. (That is, the winner is whomever gets destroyed least.) I like the way you have exactly twelve cards to play (the same twelve as everyone else), and you only get to play them once.  I like the way it's all open-info from the start: you know exactly which turns will have which disaster.
I don't like: So many complicated factors to weigh. I found myself unable to avoid AP.  I found the separate "turn order" track and pieces annoying and ad hoc. (Sure it gives you another way to win and another trade-off; but is it worth the trouble?)  In fact, I found all the multiple obtuse ways to score simply confusing. I'm surprised when reviewers say the design has no fat; for me it's inelegant and not a "clean" game.
Alternatives I'd rather play: If I want to go the multiple melds route, I'll play Ra, which simplifies the rest down so you can actually strategize. If I want the "balance complex parts, over a series of narrative rounds" I'll play Gates of Loyang, in which you similarly must plan for disaster, adjust the parts, and scrap helpers when they no longer serve you.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Such a fun "long-game" game.  Love being able to plan out more or less the whole game's strategy from the first round.  Only downside is potential imbalance of turn-order track (hard to catch up & overtake the leader) however there are workarounds that make it more balanced than it initially appears.

RATING: 6
This game has you living on the edge almost every turn. Turn order is decidedly important and the game has a method for determining and altering turn order that is tremendously original. One thing you should know, though. The game is rather unforgiving of early mistakes. There really is no "catch up" mechanic. If the first couple of disasters hit you hard, you are pretty much done.

UPDATE: Looking back over these comments, I see I never went back to update it. In the end, subsequent games weren't as tense as the first. Unless you get unlucky up front (which is unsatisfying if that's what gets you), I found the disasters pretty easy to plan for. Knocked this down a point and traded it long ago.

RATING: 9
Very nice game by the designer of Notre Dame (Stefan Feld). Some have likened this game to ND since both games involve the players avoiding disasters of some kind. In the year of the dragon gives you almost perfect information from the beginning and has you basically decide your strategy from the word go, with elements of luck causing the players to make tactical diversions throughout the game.

There seem to be many paths to victory and the game is very well balanced. I've played with 3 and 4 players and have enjoyed trying new strategies.

Update: rating moved from 8 to 9
I love this game, it offers lots of interesting decisions and subtle player interaction... as well as some overt player interaction. The game demands that you choose a strategy, but one that other's are not also pursuing. It might be a temporary jump to 9... but after 6 games I feel pretty confident that it will stay at this rating.

Good game, I quite liked this one. Although the rules are a bit overwhelming and are hard to explain, I think once people have a few plays it begins to flow nicely.

Pretty artwork, overwhelming number of options and screwage opportunities and great tension in deciding what to do on a given turn. Unfortunately, despite liking the game, I still think I would choose to play other Alea games like Notre Dame or Ra over this. Its maybe just a bit too abstract and not as fun as the other games.
