RATING: 10
After a total of four plays, I think this game is fantastic. Yes, it is quite brutal in the sense that you are going to have to lose some guys, but there are just so many tough decisions and seemingly many different ways to win.

When I was first being taught ItYotD, my friend mentioned that afterwards I wouldn't want to play Notre Dame again.  I chuckled at this because I was a fan of Notre Dame.  But afterwards, I found that to be true, Notre Dame seemed... kind of one dimensional... after experiencing Year of the Dragon.Unlike Notre Dame, almost everything is visible in ItYotD.  The only unknown is how the actions are going to be grouped.  So you are more in control of your destiny, unlike Notre Dame where you have to take what your opponents draw and give to you.  There also seems to be a wider variety of possible winning strategies than Notre Dame (though some claim that the first turn double dragon privilege is a dominant strategy).

RATING: 8
Theme is almost non existent and the game looks really dull, but the gameplay is beautiful poetry of negativity. Try to build an engine while consistently looking for which parts are potentially dropping of your engine. You also want to be doing a lot more all the time than what you are allowed to do. Add to this the constant fighting for turn order done with a brilliant mechanism and you have a tough cookie to break. The game flows quickly and it is exciting. I would like to play more.

RATING: 8

RATING: 7
Resource acquisition game where you want to have the right resources at the right times to get bonuses or avoid penalties, but so does everyone else. Player interaction is limited to action drafting and, by extension, keeping ahead in the battle for turn order, so likely to get stale if you don't like that kind of game.

Now played rather a lot against bots; it definitely works as a casual game. I can also see that there is a lot of player interaction through action hogging, much of which will amount to asymmetric and/or incidental screwage. Curious to see how this works out in a real game.

It's unfortunate that early-turn large privilege seems to be an automatic choice. The fact that people present precariously elaborate strategies to argue first-turn privilege can be beaten doesn't really convince you there isn't an issue.

RATING: 9
The Good: One of the signs of a good game is that you always want to do more on your turn than you are able, and this one does that better than almost any game I know of. Part of the reason for this is that, in addition to trying to score, there are bad events scheduled to take place throughout the game that you REALLY need to prepare for. That and several paths to victory make this one a winner. 

The Bad: Although we did not experience it, I could see the possibility for some analysis paralysis, due to the many choices each turn. I feel that the board is rather plain and that the palace tiles are too thin. Some may not like the very bad events that occur. However, they are mapped out for all to see thru-out the game, so all players can plan for them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
PROS: Multiple layers, different game every time, good player screwage, short play time, it has hookers, tax collectors can die
CONS: you won't "get it" until half way through the first go, i mean it's not too bad to explain, but you just don't immediatley realize how many strategies there are.  

The game seems to give you just enough actions to "maintain" your empire.  To "advance" your empire (get setup to start accruing more than the baseline 2VP per turn), you have to take a risk, which could end up biting you.  If you do it too early, you might be struggling to catch up the rest of the game, too late, and someone else already has the VP edge.  BUT...there isn't a single strategy that's going to work every time, because the events are randomized every GAME, though they are laid out for everyone to see, so from the start you have perfect knowledge of that aspect, and you can start formulating a strategy...but if you don't stay ahead on the player track, or pick up a person before someone else, you could get hosed.

I see high replay on this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent luck-low, brutal and unforgiving strategy game. Careful planning is crucial when deciding how to handle the different calamities (you only have 12 actions). Look to what other players are doing, a suboptimal move can wreck their plans and create havoc in their palaces (e.g. when someone really needs rice or yuans for the next event). Endscores will be very tight between players with a similar level of experience (5-player game with 1 point between first, second and third place and another 5-player game with a tied first place). Multiple paths to victory, but don't forget the buddha's for the endscore and take in account that the books can be very powerful during the year. The random sequence of the disasters ensures replayability and a quite different game play. ***** 44 plays (3/4/5 player): 27 victories & 13 second places (two ties with first player, but lower on prestige) - 4 plays (2 player): 2 wins. ***** My most impressive victory (5-player game - 4/5/2008 Delle): at the final count of the year: 6 palaces with 11 people (I only lost two people!) and 12 points from buddha's resulting in a total of 125 points (second place was at 103, third at 85, fourth at 83 and fifth at 81) - a near perfect synchronisation with the flow of events during the year. ***** Yep, finally succeeded in keeping all 13 people alive until the end! But, the event sequence was favourable and I was the only experienced player on the table (5 player game). Did this a second time (4 player game), but didn't win the game (should have bought a privilige). ***** My preferred strategy: keeping as many people as possible in the palaces until the end. This generates points during the game (as each palace is a point - each round) and ensures a lot of points at the end of the game (each person is worth 2 victory points). Having the different personalities also provides flexibility in the actions from which you can choose. Combine this with an early privilige (1 or 2 dragons depending on the events) and buddha's. This strategy beats the multiple priviliges and the books strategy (in one of my games: the person ending up in second place had 6 privilige dragons - I had only 1, but kept 11 people in 8 palaces, including four buddha's in three storey palaces [just for fun: the event in the 11th month was drought/famine and I had rice for all 8 palaces!] - enough to win with 9 points difference). ***** Belgian Champion in 2008 (Multijeux) - the final game was one of the most engaging and tough games I played - tense until the end - a very close victory, thanks to an unexpected, but crucial choice of picking a second 3 value "helmet".

RATING: 8
One of the few Stefan Feld games I like. It’s got the typical Euro mechanic that there are very few ways to screw with the other people, apart from going before them and taking an action in a group they want. (Predictably, the number of each person available for recruiting is also tied to the number of players.) Will you notice this lack of interaction? Probably not. If you’re in a room with three other guys and a tiger, and the tiger is mauling you, what the other guys are or aren’t doing is of little concern. What I’m saying here is that the game itself, the situation set up in front of you, is so tense and tough that it’s almost a relief that nobody can also set your palaces on fire or something.

I’ve played a few times, and each time I managed to survive the year without any major problems. For every event, I either had exactly what I needed to avoid the penalty, or could easily pay the price without harming myself too much. My crisis management skill was awesome. I also lost pathetically every time, because it’s not enough to do that, you also have to do something to, you know, win. You’re running a race on a treadmill, and staying the same place will require effort but it’s not going to get you to the finish line. 

More here:
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/321453/tension-and-dragons

RATING: 8
The first real quality brainburner to come from alea since Puerto Rico, and more of a true "return to form" for the big box line than Notre Dame was.

There are several interesting mechanics here&thinsp;&ndash;&thinsp;initiative, the disasters, palace management and action grouping&thinsp;&ndash;&thinsp;and they work well together to create a challenging, nuanced game, although I'd only call it moderately elegant since there [i]are[/i] a lot of them, and I'm not sure I could point to any of them, even the disasters, as the heart of the game.

There is real medium-longish term planning though, which enough to elevate this into a buy for me. It makes my brain hurt in a way that I like, and it promises to scale really well. I look forward to playing many times more. Thanks for bringing it to Canada with you, Gil & Heather.

RATING: 1
My least favorite euro game, hands down. So completely full of meaningless rules and restrictions, from the limit on starting with only certain tiles, to only being able to build houses 3 high, to only being able to buy cards a limited number of times, to tiles being bound together for paying a same-action fee. This game would be so much more fun and intuitive if you removed over half of the rules from it, and that is a terrible sign, in my opinion. Also, this is a game where if you fall behind, there is no way to recover properly. I honestly cannot say I enjoy any part of this. Beyond that, more importantly, I can't even find one part of this game that I feel is cleverly designed. That is a huge disappointment, as a game coming from the designer of Castles of Burgundy and Trajan. I can't imagine ever playing this again (currently at 3 plays).

RATING: 8
I see a game with some neat mechanics and a novel way of determining player order through the game.  The list of events and their order gives an opportunity for a level of strategy and playing off the two sides of avoiding disaster befalling your people and actually scoring enough points to win is a delicate balance.  So after two games (and in the 2nd, I scored notably more points than the first) I am liking what I have seen so far.

My wife and regular opponent has doubts about replayability - even with only 2 games she thought there was a sameness - the same events - and the importance of the 'every turn victory points' (the privileges especially) made it a race to get one of those and sit on the advantage that gave.  I am not at all convinced that is unbeatable and hope to play this a good few times.

RATING: 9
[Played more than 100 times]
Simple premises (pick actions and hire characters to get initiative and more powerful actions), but the usual goal of scoring is achieved more by dodging the nasty events than by creating an economic engine. 

PROs:
-) Fast paced.
-) Quick despite being quite strategic (about 20 minutes per player, once you know it). 
-) Good replay value due to the random order of events and actions.
-) Interesting decisions about who to recruit and how to manage efficiently the initiative track.
-) Several different strategies are workable, thus giving you much to explore.

CONs:
-) Some possibly annoying randomness with the grouping of actions each turn.
-) Extremely unforgiving.
-) Once you have made the first decisions, you may feel constrained to follow that strategy.

Better with more than 2 players (with just 2, it is quite calculable and emphasizes way too much the initiative; it's still nice, but not as good as with more).

RATING: 10
Game mechanics are superb. Slick and clean. This game brings a lot of tension and competition since there are so many things to do but so little time. And the game is not very forgiving. So its a careful sprint to victory. It doesn't take too long and experienced players will be able to play this game quickly, since there are not too many options each turn.

What I like is that this game doesn't need cards to create complexity. Your own situation, and that of your opponents is always very clear and easy to oversee. Nothing hidden, everything is open on the table. Still this is a game of great depth. 

Because for each game, the events are ordered differently, each play poses different problems and opportunities.

Typically a game that you need to play often to get better. 

Game can be played (and is fun) with any number of people up until 5

Even in 2017 I know no other game that brings more tension to the table than this one;

RATING: 9.5
The theme is really present and give a real life, with the very strong impression of living a hellish years in China. Being constantly on the razor's edge, knowing that each action could be the last one is particularly well rendered here with a good stress. The tension is palpable and intense. Surviving has never been so fun.  Even more difficult is that size and that the sacrifices that would be more painful and not easy to take. We have to find the right tempo to anticipate and survive.

Since the event are never in the same order, and your choice are always affected by other players actions. It's question of making good choices at the right time to win over but mainly to avoid losing too. This dialectic is quite interesting because it is quite different from what we usually encounter in management games. A very original design of the management spices a lot parties, making it extremely tense and difficult choice of each action because if there is one thing that does not support the players, is to know what everybody will do in their efforts development and what sacrifice their are willing to do.

RATING: 8
I've never played a game quite like it: Nearly every turn, something bad happens, and it's all about surviving the nonstop avalanche of bad shit and coming out the least badly off at the end. This is turn angst with the volume turned up -- almost every action is deliciously agonizing, and there's NEVER a perfect choice.

Every aspect of the mechanics, from the role selection to the way the people are balanced and how the disasters unfold, is superb. It's also a beautiful game, component-wise.

I've found that it's not for everyone, though. Folks in my group who normally like games of this complexity level are turned off by the core "avoid the shitslide" gameplay.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I think this is a slightly below average example of the blah that is the Eurogame. There's my usual pet peeve about artificial restrictions on purchasing items. Only one person can purchase fireworks, or rice, or whatever. It feels wrong to me, and thus is distracting, because there's no reason for it except as something the designer uses for play balance.

It is a challenging game that requires you to plan ahead and pay attention to the series of calamities/challenges ahead. Scheduled calamities, there's a concept. Ultimately, this may be the thing that turns me off the most and drops it to a 4: The "scheduled" calamities are such an artifice, so completely anti-immersive, I just don't feel like I'm doing anything. I never enter--or even approach--a state where I forget that I'm playing a game. Did I say 4? Eh, let's make it a 3.

So once again, a meh game which is an okay optimization/resource management game, with long- and short-term strategies and some screwage, but it doesn't really feel like you're doing anything. Admittedly, this is not an issue for everyone, but it is an issue for me.

I think this is one of those titles about which it is perfectly acceptable to simply say: a typical Euro. If that statement causes a strong reaction in you either way, you may already know everything you need to decide whether you would like this game.

RATING: 8
New and fresh. Great entry in the 60 minutes category -- lots of decisions, little luck, tense but not overhwelmingly so. Lots to do in 12 short turns. Unfortunately the game arc suffers a bit from the action selection mechanism, as in the later turns (especially the last 3 turns) there is much less tension in trying to figure out which character to select and which action to pick -- some of the nasty events won't be recurring so deciding which characters to get rid of is much easier, and the decision tree for which character to pick is much leaner due to having that many less character cards left in your hands. Still, a satisfying playing experience.

RATING: N/A
Ottimo x 2
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Players familiar with Feld's last game will be familiar with the rat phase at the end of each round.  Basically you need to deal with the rats or else they'll take away some of your influence and victory points.  Feld's latest design utilizes that same mechanic but increases your chance for failure by introducing different threats to contend with round by round.  You'll need to collect different commodity types depending on the upcoming threats.  The catch is that those commodities are in short supply especially considering you can only collect one type per round and your opponents are also competing for them.

The other challenge, of course, is besides trying to keep your ship afloat you need to try to squeeze in some victory points.  Problem is that this is usually to the detriment of dealing with the hazards each round.  

A challenging game with some nice mechanics.  I didn't even mention the turn-order track which is something else players compete in and really adds to the game.  Although I mentioned a ton of conflict it truly is a euro.  Some may say it's Notre Dame with meat.

UPDATE: After 3 plays this one is already losing it's appeal.  It's much longer than Notre Dame or at least it feels that way.  Knocked down a point.  UPDATE: after further play the game has revealed that it really has no catch-up mechanism.  This isn't always a huge problem but in this one it's dull to continue playing when it's a foregone conclusion or you know you don't have a chance to win half-way through.  Knocked down another 1/2 point.

RATING: N/A
Alea
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game continues the tradition from Stefan Feld (Notre Dame) of games that don't so much challenge you to gain the most of all the players, but to be impacted by disasters the least. Most of your effort is just in keeping your head above water. The winner will be the one who is able to do that and hold on to just a little bit of energy to make upward progress.

I think these games are predecessors to Agricola. In Agricola, feeding your family is a large part of the game, although perhaps not the majority, and the only purpose of it is to avoid a stiff negative penalty if you can't.

I like this one better than Notre Dame, probably because it has some more familiar Euro mechanics, and also more interaction. There's interaction in Notre Dame, but it's very subtle - pass cards you don't want, but at least secondarily, don't pass cards your opponent will want.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It take some time to understand how all the game mechanics work together. It is a game about avoiding disasters and losing points.

RATING: 5
Im Jahr der Euro-Generika! This game is ok, I even managed to do well at it, but it is another Euro game with a small array of choices that mount up to important decisions that will have a massive influence on your path through the game. And that kind of thing just doesn't appeal to me, I am generally incapable of spotting viable long term paths in abstract games. The potential variety in the 'disaster' order is probably interesting for those who will play this 10 times, but I suspect I will not be a member of that elite group. This is the same Euro concepts, just thrown together in a different order and with different bits, when will the deluge stop?

RATING: 9
I love games with lots of hard choices. This game is one of those "constantly plugging leaks" triage kinds of games--you can't conquer all the stuff the game throws at you, so you just have to make sure you come through OK and that your opponents suffer more. It has a flavour a bit like Caylus, except that unlike Caylus it has a decent randomization system (random initial setup plus the actions are grouped differently every turn) to prevent staleness. It's still not a hugely various game, though, because the 12 actual events occur invariably--it's just the ordering that changes--so multiple games can be a little samey if you don't play with expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In medieval China, players are attracting workers to their temples to survive one calendar year, where there are attacks by Mongols, drought, and contagion. In the action phase, players choose from the following: research (earn VPs through scholars), build (build a palace segment), fireworks (earn fireworks counters), money (take money), helmets (move up higher on the turn order track), rice (take rice counters), and prestige (buy a scroll to earn 1 or 2 VPs a month). These are performed in player order, and if another player wants to do another action that someone has taken, they must pay three gold. In the person phase, players recruit a worker into their temples. Each player has an 11 person deck, with 9 cards representing the unique personalities in the game, and 2 cards are wild cards. The workers are split into two categories, the younger that can do more, while the older are worth more prestige, and help the player in the action phase for turn order. Each month, there is a scoring, based on temples, number of court ladies, and scrolls a player has. At the end of the game, there is a final scoring, counting monks and 2 VPs per worker.

This is a good game, with tense decisions to be made as one tries to weather the contagions, Monguls, tribute, or drought. One has to adapt to the monthly problems, and trying to be able to stay ahead of everyone else to go first in the action phase. Fun, engaging, and each game is different, as the monthly problems are seeded differently as well. 

This game has a steep learning curve, but it is a tense game. It's different from other games in that instead of building up people or economy, you are likely to be losing people due to the calamities. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 1
Played a 4 player and a 5 player. I found the game rigid and agonizing. Unlike some of Feld's other games (CoB, Macao), this one doesn't seem to have the same freedoms of action in terms of paths to victory. In fact, if you are last or second to last in the fluctuating turn order, you're not going to win (at least I don't think so), so you must take person cards that assure you a 2nd, or better yet, first choice the majority of the time. In addition, because the calamities affect everyone, players tend to want the exact same actions at the same time. The game feels crowded and narrow as a result, with decisions forced upon each player at a tactical level; worse, both times I played I didn't find it fun. I do want to give this game another shot to explore different strategies, but I'm worried it'll be more satisfying as a thought exercise than in the actual playing. [Comes with Great Wall & SuperEvents expansion for trade]

Finally played it again, 3p. Since being first matters so much, the game probably plays best with 2 or 3 -- especially if people know what to do (i.e., they've either discovered the winning formula, or read about it). The winning formula being, pick 6 monk and 5 warrior/5 firework in order to go first, then choose double privilege to gain a 2 point advantage. The game remains tightly scripted from there, with variations only arising based upon the initial layout of the events and which actions get grouped together. It's not a bad experience if you're the one who gets that initial advantage; it's hell if you're one of the other players, especially in a 5p game, as you sit helplessly watching the gap grow wider between the leader and you. Maybe with 2p it can be more of a match.
----
keeping, but only for completism

RATING: 8

Update (3.10.2010): The game works somewhat as 2-player game but most of the game's annoyances are enhanced when played like this. More stuff is blocked by pure luck when 1. player chooses his pile and turn order is therefore extra important.

RATING: 8
The quality evident in both the design and the imagery make this another solid winner from Alea.The game mainly has the feel of barely treading with one head above the water. There are lots of potentials in what to do, but only a limited number of actions with which to do them. The game is balanced between trying to go earlier within a turn and thus claiming the action you want versus going later, but being able to potentially do more with that action. Also, certain actions need to be taken to offset a disastrous event (8 of the 12) or to try and benefit from a beneficial event (2 of the 12). It is this precarious balance between short-term benefits versus long-term needs that gives the game its tension and also puts it head and shoulders above most of the other Euros.I can easily see this one becoming one of my top three games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There seems to be a couple of constant threads through Stefan Feld's output. In the Year of the Dragon illustrates them extremely well, and does so without necessarily resorting to an overwhelming amount of material:
* A SIMPLE CORE IDEA. Players must allocate different workers within their palaces (which can be expanded as well) to protect themselves against a series of disasters that will happen throughout the game: each of the months of a year every player will choose an action and recruit a worker using cards. A player's hand only allows to recruit one worker of each kind and two free choices; two more workers are chosen for the setup. 
* MANY OPTIONS FOR SEVERAL ASPECTS IN THE GAME. There are five different deadlines that happen twice throughout the year, seven different actions and nine kinds of workers, each of them with different implications and some of them divided in seniors and juniors.
* NOT ENOUGH TIME TO MEET ALL THE DEADLINES.

The result is, as frequently stated for Stefan Feld, a rich and cerebral game that takes one try to assimilate, two or three to have fun with and two hundred to play really well. There are multiple possible strategies, which work differently with a different amount of players, and only the setup order can be called random. The turn order mechanic (a secondary scoring track) is the game's secret weapon: the available actions are few, and if they have been taken before in the turn they're also costly. This forces players to balance as carefully as possible between picking up strong actions and securing early slots. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Probabilmente il più bel gioco di Feld di sempre. Un gioco semplicissimo ma profondo e estremamente punitivo. Il gioco dura 12 turni ed in ogni turno bisogna svolgere 4 fasi:
1. Scegliere una tessera azione
2. Giocare una carta e prendere il relativo lavoratore
3. Risolvere l'evento (solitamente negativo)
4. Assegnare i punti vittoria.
Gli eventi sono noti dall'inizio della partita e i giocatori cercheranno di costruire la loro strategia in base all'ordine di successione. Un gioco molto interessante e cattivo. Promosso a pieni voti.

OTHERS HAVE IT? No.
WANT? Yes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an extremely tight worker placement game about mitigating the disasters that will hit you every round starting turn 3, while still trying to maintain a good point income. Each round you take the player actions and divide them into groups equal to the number of players, which means sometimes when you take an action you make a choice between two possibilities. Then each person gets to play a card allowing them to select one of the people tiles to add to their tableau. These people either help you mitigate the events, or get you points. Then you resolve the punishing event effect that forces you to potentially discard your people titles and then you score some points. This game can be very unforgiving of mistakes and you need to keep up with other player's point generation engines. There isn't really a catchup mechanism in this game, which can feel a little hopeless for the players not doing well. Despite this, it's a tight, punchy game full of suffering which is something I can appreciate.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 9
I doubt I will ever consider playing Notre Dame again, as this is a superior game with similar mechanics. The games mechanics are easy to understand. This is a game of crisis management. One mistake can totally unravel your whole game and destroy you utterly as I learned the hard way. I can't think of many other euros that are this unforgiving of mistakes, except maybe age of steam. Yet it keeps you coming back for more. There is an element to this game that makes you feel like you're playing against the game not the other players. A highly enjoyable time. We've seen people win from the front and back in turn order, however it is much more difficult from the back. There is an imbalance to the game from the first turn onwards in that the last player to go (determined randomly) can take a character combination that will assure him frist place in turn order and buy a double privilage. That is 24 points as a result of a random draw.

RATING: 7.5
This seems like a complicated game due to the many ways to recieve points (pt) to win.  You receive points by the following means:  1 pt for each castle per turn, 1 pt for each cortison per turn, 1 pt for each script (buy 1 or 2 pt script) per turn and at the end of the game you get 1 pt for every three sets of items or coins, 2 pts for every worker and 1 pt per castle floor you have a monk in it.

I was behind by 10 points and still won with last turn results: (10 workers x 2 pts = 20 pts; castle with 3 floors (max of 3 floors) x 1 monk pt = 3 pts; 4 castles x 1 pt = 4 pts; 1 cortison x 1 pt = 1 pt; 3 fireworks/3 = 1 Pt and 9 Yen/3 = 3 pts, for a total of 32 pts received on the last turn.).

RATING: 6
Place the ten disasters/opportunities on the track in a random order. Each turn players choose an action and a particular type of loyal follower. These interact to generate food, fire-works, victory points, etc. This is a planning game, in which you look ahead and try to work out what you will need and when, and optimise it against the production of victory points.
The game is a bit fiddly on your first play, but after that the choices are fairly simple (you have only two on each turn, one is a choice of seven actions in which usually some are clearly not worthwhile, and one is a choice is essentially choosing an order in which a set of cards are resolved over the game). 
It can be a bit lacking in flavour but it deserves more plays to establish how much depth there is to the strategy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A fun game. Easy to learn rules. Nice cards and pieces. Logical. Captures the flavor of the East.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game was long on the wishlist of my wife and I (we're both big Feld fans, but only got into gaming in 2011), but it is long out of print and totally unavailable, at least in the UK. Luckily, my wife managed to buy an unused German-language edition from a BGG user in December 2013 - the components are language-independent and English rules are available online, so it is completely playable.

We've played a few times, all shortly after getting it, and all with just 2 - it is an interesting game, and is definitely recognisable as a Feld design. It does show its age a little, I feel, but is still very enjoyable - once you know the game then 2-player has very little downtime, it is very replayable as the order of events makes a big difference to how one approaches things, and I would very much like to try this with more than 2 players (although I also think it's a very good 2-player game). But that still hasn't happened, nor have my wife and I played it again as a 2p, since those first few games in 2013 - there are too many other games we want to play, and the fact that we don't have printed English rules to refer to doesn't help.

RATING: 7.2
(+) Very elegant game: a lot of game compared to the rather small set of rules

(+) Very flexible: the game is easily explained, does not take long to play, is accessible to casual gamers yet also satisfying for "hardcore gamers".
(+) Very low/no luck-factor
(+) Considering this is a Feld-title, the theme is ok

(-) Game material is really, *really* crappy
(-) Chosing the right starting cards seems very crutial and this might benefit the random turn order in the set-up phase.

Approx gametime: 30 min / player

RATING: 8
The game takes place over twelve rounds, each representing a month on the year. The first two months are "peaceful," and then subsequent months have randomized events, two each of: war, famine, drought, plague and (for a nice change) fireworks. You can see which order these will happen in, allowing you to plan out a strategy for the game. Each player has two, two-story pagodas, allowing them to hire four people (one per story). Each person they have enhances the actions they take. For instance, if you take the Farm Rice action, you'll produce 1 rice tile (in preparation for famine), but if you have a Rice Farmer in your house, you'll produce more. The game has two tracks, a scoring track and a turn order track, which is kind of interesting. Acquiring people affects your advance on the turn order track. There are young and old versions of most people. The young people produce less, but they advance you more. The old people do the reverse.

Turn order comes into play when you take your actions. There are seven actions, and they're randomly split into N groups (where N is the number of players). In player order, you select an action and block out the whole group. Going last in turn order means you'll be forced to take an action you don't want, or you'll have to pay cash to force your way into a previously selected group to get the action you want. Some of these actions are let you build (adding more stories to your pagodas or adding more pagodas -- this action is enhanced by having builder people living in your pagodas already), make fireworks (having fireworks people living in your house helps you produce more of them), make money (enhanced by moneychangers in your house), and move farther on the turn order track (enhanced by having warriors in your house, which are also good for when war comes around).

The events are resolved each turn, some of the event consequences are more dire than others, but they usually involve killing off some of the people in your house or taking your money. This is interesting game about long and short term planning. You know you war is coming, so you want to have more warriors in your house than your opponents by the time in gets here. You see both plague events are hitting one after another, so there will be a mad rush to pick up healers at that time -- maybe pick them up in advance to avoid the rush? But then you run out of space in your pagodas and have to evict someone when you pick up a new person for your house, so you've got to build. But building goes faster if you have builders in your house, so you could pick them up first, then build, then evict them when the time comes and you need the space...

I like how this game has many strategies for victory, and multiple things to balance. Most of the interaction is indirect, except for the action selection.

UPDATE: Moving from 7 to 8. The long term planning enabled by seeing the random events all laid out for the year makes deep strategy possible. Very nice.

RATING: 6

A lot of digesting the mechanisms of arbitrary people and actions that do arbitrary things to collect arbitrary resources and score arbitrarily, and the pay-off isn't really worth it, because once you get past the "language", you and your opponents can tend to just optimize given the current situation. The game seems to just artificially encourage situations that leave you f***ed, and labels it "tension". Also very dubious about game balance. The double-Privilege early might not be broken, but it's annoying in that everybody should jump on it or him if somebody does it. Scholars are pathetically weak.

More on the artifice of the game: the closing up of random action choices if you choose certain actions is an artificial way to introduce player interaction into a "game" whose soul is really about optimizing against the board, not a whole lot against other players.

The initiative track is cool, but not enough to rescue the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
I've borrowed this from our local Feldian, to see if atypical Feld would do something form me. Turns out: mean Feld is still Feld.

It's a MPS game of optimising scarce actions (12 per game + 12 buys). While it seems half of them give you points and half prevent you from losing points (or parts of your VP engine), the difference isn't that big. It's an action selection game, kinda streamlined, kinda fiddly, theme is kinda there and isn't. The only difference between old and new Feld is an emotional illusion of losing points VS getting less point. Mostly it's obvious what you need to do, it's just in the timing.

At the end of day it's only an optimising puzzle. Thing is, our Feldian doesn't like it, and frankly this is so bland, so accountant-y, I'm surprised anybody actually gets excited by it. But they do apparently. Picture me confused.

Down 0.5 a point - because optimisation within a minimal framework is a special circle of hell.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For [i]In the Year of the Dragon[/i], Feld takes two of his favoured design elements – penalty avoidance and competition for turn order – and dedicates an entire game to them. The result is an unconventional design in a positive sense. Not only is the game an unabashed struggle for turn order, but it also tinkers with players' loss aversion tendencies. 

What appeals to me is how the game requires you to gradually dismantle your hard-earned empire – often for the greater good. Many Euros focus on building and running your engine, but no other game comes close to capturing this design's brutal yet zen-like atmosphere of taking apart components which are no longer useful to you. (A general tip, though, would be to prepare for events two months ahead in advance.)

Having played over 15 games, there is one issue that continues to bug me. Because of the way the various restrictions are set up, there are several points in the game when mathematical logic practically dictates your next move. (Assuming, of course, that you are playing to win.) There is very little room for unconventional maneuvers, and the feeling of constant constraint becomes tiring after a while.

RATING: 7.5
Alea is almost trying to make amends for the sorry Rum and Pirates. Stefan Feld (Alea ‘it’ boy) follows up Notre Dame with this cracker of a ‘gamers game’. Players are trying to draft actions which allow them to build up their kingdom. Different types of people are recruited to help create various things needed to gain VPs or stave off calamities and events at the end of each year (turn).

The game is all about trying to scores VPs while trying to manage the (generally) horrible effects of events. There is constant jockeying for position and money is tight. Mechanics are tightly intervoven so that players are constantly trying to find the best balance between recruiting people and using their benefits. One has to constantly focus on what everyone else is doing. Very intense. Only downside is that the package looks like an advanced prototype more than an actual product…bring back Vohwinkle please.

RATING: 6
It is a solid action drafting game (players chose one at a time from the available actions). The problem is that I'm on the verge of saturation when it comes to action drafting.  After El Grande came out, everyone was trying to use its Area Control system.  Now that Area Control is played out everyone has looked back to El Grande for more things to take, with Caylus it was action drafting.  Because Caylus was so successful, it seems that everyone is using it now. Agricola is heavily steeped in it, but it also is running on the other hot design mechanic the Engine System.  With In the Year of the Dragon, it is all drafting and action drafting.  It works well, and because the game beats you up so much it is engaging, but come on, can we give action drafting a rest.

About the game itself, well, it is annoying that at a certain point in the game (halfway?) some players will be well aware that there will be no way for them to win.  I don't mind it in some games, but here this game is all about point accumulation.  It is so abstract that I can't just focus on some element of your game for fun and just hope to place as best as I can.  Certainly experience will yield the best success, but that makes me wonder how balanced it is...

I don't know, its fine.  What isn't fine is the graphic design of the board.  I'm not sure why there even is a board.  I guess it is supposed to be a carpet?  If just looks like someone printed out orange on a sheet of paper only to discover it isn't water resistant ink when they spilled their Mountain Dew on it.  It looks like it was produced straight from the prototype.  Ugly and uninspired.  I wish they would have put a beautiful Chinese landscape on it.  Maybe, have the scoreboard be the Great Wall?  Perhaps the whole thing as a top view of the Forbidden City? Blech.

RATING: 7
Interesting Game.

Pros:
* interesting mechanic of determining the start player each round - a separate 'person score' that is increased when choosing tiles, so there is a constant tension between the tile and the next round position.
* Many ways to score points - women tiles, privileges, events (dragon festival), monk tiles.
* Tight in the sense that each player has exactly the same cards for the whole game, so require planning.
* Accessible to the girls (no english in game).

Cons:
* Quite fiddly, lots of rules. Actually the rules for the events are quite arbitrary - some give bonus points, some punish by taking stuff away. Hard to remember, require constant reference to the board.

RATING: 6.5
An interesting game that I would expect to like more than I actually did. It's a resource management game: you get 2 turns to build up some resources, then in every following turn you can try adding to your resources, but must face some event that will usually take some of those resources away. These disasters are set up (and public) at the beginning, so you know the order in which they will occur, giving you some idea of which resources you should go after, in which order. Still, there seems to be room for different strategies to get VP while minimizing your losses. Unfortunately, our first game just wasn't all that much [i]fun[/i]. I like the ideas here, but you don't go into the game expecting to kill your people off willy nilly. You expect to play every person in your hand and end the game with a huge court! At least, I was hoping to -- most efficiency games allow you to keep most of what you play. Of course, that isn't the case here. I'd guess that if you really KNEW that up front, it would feel better; but it just didn't feel good trying to optimize things and still lose stuff! I also like the person track. Given the way the action selection works and how tight money is, turn order manipulation is VERY important.

RATING: 8
I quite like this game, there's nothing else in my collection like it.  The gameplay is simple enough to teach quickly, almost anybody can pick it up, and some of the mechanics are quite original.  I especially like the person track that determines play order and the balance between younger and older person tiles and how it relates to moving along the person track.

This is almost a game just of damage control.  The game will kick your butt from beginning to end, there is no way to completely mitigate all the negative events.  And even if you could then you wouldn't be scoring points...which of course is how you win the game.  Every round is tense as you try to balance number of palaces, 9 different person tiles, money, position on the person track, mitigating events, and scoring points.  You can also play this game a different way every time and still win.  The components are also stylish and attractive and the theme is pretty persistent and interesting.

I like the tension and brutality of this game because it is never just one player who is the victim of it but unfortunately that is exactly what might keep this game from hitting the table as often as I'd like.  Not everyone likes to be beat up by their games, whether they have company or not.

RATING: 5.5
This is a very interesting, well-made, tension-filled, medium complexity game with multiple paths to victory that is not my style.  Learning the rules of the game is not too big a challenge, but learning HOW to play it is.  The mechanics of the game are well designed, and the production value is fairly average.  Unfortunately the fun factor just isn't there for me (and it's NOT just the lack of theme - there are plenty of fairly abstract games that I enjoy).  This is one of those games that just kicks your ass and leaves you feeling like you are continuously on the edge of disaster.  I can see how some would relish the challenge and would find the tension quite exciting.  Myself, I would rather play a game in which I feel like I am progressing and accomplishing something, rather than simply minimizing losses.  So not a bad game, just not for me.  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 2/5  Total: 5.5/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Surprised I hadn't left a comment for this one already. Older Feld game most notable for its disaster track: at the end of each round, players can be punished by various afflictions (e.g. a plague that wipes out part of the population if healers are not present, a famine that does the same without rice, an emperor who does the same without tribute). There is also a fireworks event, resulting in victory points instead of death.

Part of the game's charm is in how differently it plays out with different disaster ordering -- for example, if two plagues are in quick succession then it is safe to let healers subsequently die as their services are no longer required. There's quite a bit of point-salad to it, with several different types of both in-game and end-game scoring.

Most people I play with are big fans but I don't understand the appeal. Part of that is a personal bias against unthematic spreadsheet games with no spatial element. But my biggest qualm is the mechanism where actions each turn are randomly divided into groups; the first player to visit a group gets their action free and those subsequent must pay. This drives pleasantly strong jockeying for turn order, which also informs the choice of citizens -- those that provide more in-game benefit do less for turn order. The issue is that the randomness of the groups has a very strong impact on later players, particularly the second in turn, who may or may not also get their favoured choices for free. Money is so tight that this is no small bonus.

I first gave this an 8 because I really enjoyed it, but decided that was probably a bit hasty after just one play, so I changed that to a 7. After my second play, a little of the shine has worn off, but it's still a great game. I'm sticking with the 7.

RATING: 7
[RGG 'In the Year of the Dragon' 2007 edition] {China; action-draft, variable-player-order, resource management; optimal 5p} YoD is a solid euro game.  In the core, it's a traditional Euro game - calculation, calculation and calculation.  However, it adds some refreshment to traditional euro game - your VP engine is subject to brutal destruction.

It is a gamer's game - abstract and full of calculation.  The theme is thin (does not couple with the mechanics well).  It is a tense resource management game. Players usually would like to do more than 1 thing in a turn.  However, the game is not highly vulnerable to analysis paralysis and a game turn can usually be completed within 5-7 minutes.  The game is clearly well tested and nicely balanced.  Luck is very low (nearly open information); however, the strategy is pre-set at early games and players can hardly change their strategy during the game; thus it is not a reactive game.  Replayability is good for the random event track and random grouping of action cards.  It's unforgiving - early mistakes can make one have no chance of winning.  Player interaction is moderate - no direct confrontation but the competition on initiation track, actions, and person tiles are strong.

The rule is not complex and can easily be understood.  However, there is a learning curve here.  I think players need 4-10 games to explore all potential scoring methods and counter methods.

There are a few minuses of YoD that make it an only good game, not a great one.  First, the theme is tenuous.  Mechanics are important but a great game should be one with nicely coupled mechanics and theme.  Next, it lacks some strong excitement.  It is fun for gamers who enjoy winning with a better strategy.  If players are experienced with all scoring and countering methods.  They only random factor is the grouping of action cards - which is the only way to add excitement.  And there is no way of planning an intrigue to ambush / grab a large amount of score due to the nearly open-information nature.  There are no surprises (in mid / late game).  Finally, the loser-losing-more problem is very acute and it usually happens.  It is very discouraging if players know that they have no chance of winning in mid-game.  This is not a good sign.

In all, it is a solid and fun game for a group of experienced gamers, who know the game well.

RATING: 8
The game does offer many viable paths to victory; however, it seems that one or two paths (depending on the event track) are far, far superior to others and really determine the game in the first 3 or 4 turns. I wish the game was less "harsh" and did not have so many compounding bonuses that are insanely good to get at the start and near useless at the end. Overall it was a fun game and reminded me of Caylus with the different paths to victory. 

Suggestions for improving the game: Reduce starting money from 6 to 5 to prevent a rush for 2-victory point a turn "Privileges." Also, Geishas (Ladies) needs a higher "People" value (from 1 to 3?) or something else, they are very weak and rarely do much in a game compared to other early game choices. Another idea would be to reduce the cost of paying the Emperor Tribute by 1 for each Lady, which could balance her compared to everyone else.

House Rule: Possibly Geishas (Court Ladies) reduce the cost you have to pay to the Emperor by 1 for each one you have. Seems to make them much more useful and balanced compared to all the other pieces.

EDIT: Slightly lower in score as the game can be kingmakery if people are not experienced players. Such as overly valuing things like fireworks. Also, there is a bit of randomness that can completely screw someone over in some elements.

EDIT 2: Again, lowered slightly due to the problem with lack of people keeping others in check. Also, at the highest level it seems to come down just to action layout. Need to try with the expansions to see if they make the game better.

RATING: 8
Superior worker placement game. It's very simple to understand and quick to learn but still provides a good deal of strategy. What I find most appealing about the game is balancing the decision of becoming the first player to make moves, which obviously provides you with more choices making the game easier (although there's perhaps a bit too much emphasis on this aspect of the gameplay - if you're continually picking last you aren't going to win), or going for victory points. There are negative consequences from making poor decisions, which is a feature I know some people don't like about many games of this type. The production values are pretty standard Euro-fare (nothing to get too excited about), although I do like the Chinese-themed artwork, which complements the game's theme very well.

RATING: 10
I love this game. It's perfect.

Agonizing decisions every round. Nothing is easy. Very difficult to decide what to do since all options give you something, but you miss something else equally important. You never get everything you need. Information is there on the board, you know what is coming, but you can't prepare for every catastrophe, since focusing on one doesn't give you enough time to prepare yourself for another. Minimize your losses. Despite how it sounds, playing can be very satisfactory.

Event tiles are shuffled and randomly placed at set up. That means replayability, as you don't need same things in same order as before.

You don't know what turn angst is before you have played this game. :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Perhaps my favorite Feld! -- and that's saying something for me. The heavy point salad is missing (even if there are echoes of it hiding in some places). If anything, this rather abstract game feels true to Feld's aesthetic. It's about generating lots of points, but it wears this trait on its sleeve, and doesn't hide behind any chrome.

Also helping is the constantly changing landscape of disasters that you must deal with in order to avoid penalties. These disasters drive the play experience and lend the game a certain dynamism that is frustrating in a very enjoyable way. At the same time, you want to figure out a way to generate points round after round in a reliable way, or save up for a large cache of points in the end-game scoring. With so many moving parts, it's easy to see how Feld transitioned into designs like Trajan and Bora Bora, but here the insanity is held in check by a theme (abstract as it is) that is consistent throughout.

At its heart, Dragon is an action selection/card game that lacks any sense of positional play. For whatever reason, that lack of place combined with the round-to-round scoring mechanisms, made the first few turns feel very opaque. The good news is that this feeling of being lost subsided as the point system gelled in my mind by about round three or four. It's a game that you have to play at least twice to fully understand, but the first time will still be enjoyable thanks to the disaster track, which provides easy to understand goals.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like this game.  Yes, it feels brutal, as there are so many things you need, and yet you can only get one thing per turn.  But it's the good kind of brutal.  The order of the events are known to everyone, which aids in planning.  However, other players need the same thing as you at those known times, so there can be a lot of fighting over the same resources.  I love the tension involved as players build up, lose workers, slide by disasters at the last second, and so on.  Another great Alea game.

I also love working out what disasters you should simply ignore, tempted by points or the threat of an even bigger, looming disaster. Plan, manage, try to stay a float and even thrive. Great game.

RATING: 8.75
Excellent game with plenty of paths to victory.  Each action presents many choices, all of which seem to have their pluses and minuses.  One of a slew of recent games (along with Notre Dame, Agricola & Galaxy Trucker) in which you must keep one eye on scoring points and the other on just surviving.

Alea expansion adds a nice touch with the Super Event, which gives yet another wrinkle to the event order (which already gives a lot of replayability to the game).  The Great Wall seems to add a safety-valve and make play a little "looser", which is probably necessary when the Super Event is negative, but I'm not sure I like it when the Super Event is neutral or positive.

RATING: 8
[thing=31594][/thing] was one game I wanted to play for quite a long time - but its OOP status lasted for several years and simply no one I play with had it. Until one did. And now I have opinions.

[thing=31594][/thing] is a work placement game from Stefan Feld, before he started his thing on colored dice and giving points for everything. In here you still gain points 16 times or more times during play and one extra final scoring, so there is no shortage of points, as this is Feld, after all, but one meaner and wanting to kill some folk and destroy houses.

The complexity of [thing=31594][/thing] doesn't come from mix of mini games or bucket of rules, is placed, instead, in the most proper place: in the decision-making process: there are 8 months with problems (diseases, famine, taxes or attacks), two with extra scoring (the festivities), and you players simply won't be able to prepare for everything. Choices will have to be made, compromises accepted and dealt with. Workers will die/leave/slaved, maybe you will lose floors on your houses, and points will go to others and you will just be able to look and think "ah, I wish it was me".

Playing time, surprisingly, is much more manageable and lean than the informed 75-100 minutes - we normally don't pass the 75 mark with 4, and stay around 60 with 3. Maybe with 5 players taking their time, sure, is possible, and is best to shoot above than bellow in this matter. Still, is important to mention that the pacing is fast, as though there are multiple decisions each turn, they are often done quick.

While I don't like everything Feld has done (Arena: Rome is neat, but too agressive for my taste; and Oracle of Delphi was disappointing; etc), I do enjoy most of them, considering some brilliant games (Macao, Strasbourg, Notre Dame, etc), and [thing=31594][/thing] is up there with the best: smart, tense, with interaction (denial, non-destructive), though decisions all around, punitive, yes, but giving points in all turns, to make players feel better, lively pace with small downtime - put all of this together and, sir, you got yourself a classic. Highly recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Torn. I like the game, especially in theory. It plays fast and is rather simple. It has interesting drafting and you care somewhat what other players are doing. But on the other side it feels a little "loose" and unpolished, and the theme is slightly odd.

On a turn you first draft to take one of seven actions. The actions are divided randomly into a number of groups equal to the number of players, so it's like you have one fat worker who blocks other actions besides the one he's taking. Afterwards you draft specialists from a limited supply, using limited cards (i.e. you may run out of opportunities to draft a given type) and often can choose between a better specialist, or a worse one who will improve your turn priority.

Thematically I most dislike that three of the nine specialists just give you VP, and there's also an action that does this as well. So, four thematically and mechanically distinct things that just give VP. That reeks of a spreadsheet game.

The main theme is that there are twelve months, i.e. twelve turns, in each of which there will be some disaster or event that players can see coming and prepare for. While it is only slightly odd that players should be able to foresee when disasters will strike, there are some distracting side effects of this. The most obvious case is food. Drought will strike twice, and this is the only time you need food. If the droughts are over, or if you've accumulated enough food to survive them, you can safely get rid of your farmer specialists and never take the food action again.

(There are similar but lesser issues with the other events: The festivals' effect on the need for fireworks specialists (lesser issue because the amount you need depends on what others have); plague's effect on the need for healers (lesser issue because you need to have the healers at the time of the plague, and there is no draftable action associated with healers); and "tribute" with money (lesser issue because money has another important use). The last event is Mongol invasion, after which you don't need to keep your warriors around, though they can also be used to improve your turn order.)

The Mongol invasions provoke an arms race reminiscent of the same thing in 7 Wonders (or the Speicherstadt with its firemen), except that the number of military specialists is limited to the point that by the time the second Mongol invasion is coming around, there may be nothing left to draft, so you can only shrug and accept whatever the foregone conclusion is.

Ultimately it's a pretty good game (fast and with not a lot of superfluous junk) but noticeably weird and flawed.

...I'm surprised there are people that have a problem with this game knocking down their sandcastles... I thought it was rather mild compared to Pax Porfiriana or even Peloponnes. Maybe with more players it's a bigger issue? I played with three but suspect it's better with more.

...I'm excited about the [i]type[/i] of euro that this is: No spatial element, no deluge of special powers or tiles, and it doesn't feel like MPS. Need more like this.

...Played again with 4 players and also the Great Wall expansion. One player bought a large privilege right away, which contributed to faring very poorly with the events. They managed to maintain a lead for quite awhile, even when completely obliterated, but ultimately placed last, having no people. So, now I understand how one could find the game too harsh. It has euro-style player elimination.

That said, I definitely enjoy playing the game and am upping the score a bit and putting it on my top ten.

...Another thing I like about the game is how it tends to promote diversity of player positions.

RATING: 7
layers take on the role of Chinese rulers around the year 1000. The game plays out in twelve rounds, with each round representing one month in a year that seems to go from bad to worse. Disease, drought, and attacks from the Mongols may claim lives, but make sure you have enough money to offer a tribute to the Emperor.

The game play is easier than it may appear. Every player has a set of "person" cards. Each round, you choose one action (most of which call on your workers' abilities) help you prepare for the months ahead. Then you play one person card, recruiting that person and placing him into one of your palaces. Each person brings different skills and abilities to help you ride out the year. (Farmers help you gain rice to survive a drought month, Tax Collectors raise money, etc.) At the end of each round, that month's event is triggered, which may cost you some of your workers, some money, or give you points.

Careful planning is the key to surviving "the year of the dragon," but survival alone may not win you the game.

RATING: 10
After my strong impression of Castles of Burgundy, I had high expectations for Stefan Feld's games, but I still managed to be amazed when I played In the Year of the Dragon. I really enjoy the great planning and strategy required to avert disaster, while still scoring decently. Just a fantastic game, and quite possibly my favorite. 

Edit: After several more face to face games and 13 games on Board Game Arena, I can confidently say that this is a fantastic game. I have seen an extreme variety of winning strategies- high initiative strategy with lots of helmets, craftsman strategy, tax collector and privilege strategy, scholar strategy, and even a very successful court lady strategy.  The only drawback is that it is not a good two-player game at all because the initiative track is too important. However, it is still quite good with 3 players, and is phenomenal with 4 or 5.

Just forget this game even has a 2-player version, and it's awesome. I kept playing 2 player on Board Game Arena because I so badly want it not to be broken, but I haven't figured out a way to beat an initiative strategy that revolves around helmets, so the game is decided based on who picks tiles first. The second player may have a chance if they get lucky enough with the remaining action choices being ones they want to perform, but in my opinion, this causes the game to lose its charm, since one of my favorite parts of the game are the widely varied paths to victory. I can't recommend it enough with more players, though.

I've won a a runaway victory by hanging back with one group, but then I've also gotten killed by trying the same thing with another.  The strategy is remarkably fluid.  I like the emphasis on mid-range strategy to try to set yourself up to mitigate calamities three or four steps down the road.  The person track also keeps you thinking every game.  You can really be hurt if you hang back too much and allow yourself to be vulnerable to other players.

RATING: N/A
Players take on the role of Chinese rulers around the year 1000. The game plays out in twelve rounds, with each round representing one month in a year that seems to go from bad to worse. Disease, drought, and attacks from the Mongols may claim lives, but make sure you have enough money to offer a tribute to the Emperor.
\13\\13\
The game play is easier than it may appear. Every player has a set of "person" cards. Each round, you choose one action (most of which call on your workers' abilities) help you prepare for the months ahead. Then you play one person card, recruiting that person and placing him into one of your palaces. Each person brings different skills and abilities to help you ride out the year. (Farmers help you gain rice to survive a drought month, Tax Collectors raise money, etc.) At the end of each round, that month's event is triggered, which may cost you some of your workers, some money, or give you points.
\13\\13\
Careful planning is the key to surviving "the year of the dragon," but survival alone may not win you the game.

RATING: 9
A totally underrated crisis management game and one of the most unique euros I've ever played. Maximum depth, minimum rules. Every decision made in this game is critical and deliciously agonizing. You have to plan your actions very carefully and really stick to a long-term strategy. One mistake made early on in the game might have dramatic consequences.

Gotta say that this game gets better and better with every subsequent play, especially when you play with skilled, demanding opponents (I emphasise the plural here - you need at least 3 players, this game just isn't nearly as interesting when played only with 2). Try it out if you like strategy-heavy games like Caylus.

Just remember to play with the official anniversary edition rules change: large privilege now costs 7 juan instead of 6! This really balances out the opening moves.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Been watching for awhile - I've already posted my initial component thoughts as a review.  Will probably collect thoughts on the gameplay here until I have enough for a review.

Feels like an engine building game,  but with the constant events,  it's more a juggling game, since you have to keep switching parts of your engine to keep up with events.

Interaction can feel indirect,  but blocking the "essential" actions of those without money is pretty nasty.

No luck means that it's a pretty unforgiving design.   Miscalculations can be pretty costly,  since there's reset - good investments pay off a lot, and mistakes compound.

Remembering what all the events do is a bit tough - would have been nice to have this information on the tiles themselves.

A little effort to setup/take down - need to sort all the different types of people into their stacks.  18,  which isn't as many as the Puerto Rico buildings,  and the upside is there's different colour tiles and didfferent pictures for all of them.

It's a bit of a cold-hearted game,  last month's heroes are often this month's cannon fodder.  Then again,  there's hilarious bits of narrative as people toss out the pious monks in favour of glittery dancing girls.

Usability:  Add a spot on the board for the "refill money action".   Hints/Icons on the action tiles of what the event is about.   Icons on the younger tiles matching the bottom row (and reminder that you only take these to start).

I have had one game with a "fallaway trailer" issue.  Most games I've played have been pretty close to the end between the leaders,  but it is definitely very possible to fall behind,  especially if you have no money.

Wow...seems like it's really easy to play this one wrong.  Looks like you *do* have to lose people during drought from specific pagodas,  not amortised food loss.

RATING: 9
I started to rate this a perfect 10, but decided to start at 9 since we've only played it once.  And it's quite unique in that it's an amalgam of mechanics - it's a civilization building game, but fits in crisis management, turn management, resource management, and varied short-term goals, where you have to find some balance to score well.  Because the timing of the goals are random, there is no single strategy that will always win.  The randomly-timed goals also eliminate repetition that often accompanies games, because in one turn you'll need to acquire fireworks, but in another you'll need to accumulate enough wealth to pay the tax man. RATING: 4
I should like this one.... lots of long term planning or "bad stuff" happens.  After 5 plays, I can say I don't like this game & I've come to realize the 2 things that I really dislike:  

1) If you make a mistake (e.g. don't have enough money to take an action that has already been selected), it's pretty much game over. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
{ P - 3 4 [5] }

We had competitive games of Dragon for a while with various strategies winning ... then someone discovered 6/5. After two people jumped on board, all of our games began to look very similar. Our starting positions became:

1. 6/5 Soldier
2. 6/5 Fireworks
3. Ho-Builder
4. Tax-Builder
5. Farmer-Builder

Some tenets:

[b]Points Matter, People Don't[/b]: Points per turn is everything for the first ten or so months, so the earlier the better for annuity benefits. It just so happens those months are peaceful, making them an ideal opportunity for starting this process. 6/5's will alternate privilege and build actions during those months for 5 points per turn by the second month. Ho-Builder doubles up build action and Lady recruitment unless Drought is early. 7 points per turn. (Else, 5 points per turn.) Any other Builder start can pay to build once or twice, potentially recruit another Builder for double-builds, and also potentially recruiting Ladies. Alternatively, Tax-Builder can build once, recruit a Tax Collector, (pay to) tax, then recruit a Lady.

[b]Other Setups Invalidated[/b]: Competing with 6/5 for high initiative is futile. 6/5 + 6 secures first and second highest initiative on the two turns that matter most, barring a 5/5 Soldier start and gambling for unpaired parade, which still only nets you 4 points per turn if successful. The start does restrict both 6/5's to 4 points per turn but you lose the building war because on the turn 6/5 Soldier is crowded out of build he faces the same parade gamble as you to surpass your initiative and swipe the turn three build. Assuming both work out, you're in worse shape. Other setups without Builder or Lady cannot score enough points per turn to compete. You can't really start with, say, Farmer Apothecary because the 6/5's take Privilege and Build and you're left hoping for unpaired Food, which is still a subpar action as it does not contribute to VP per turn. Moreover, you need to build at some point for capacity so there's no reason not to begin with a Builder and start early. Fireworks and Scholar, the straight VP options are also poor early choices weighed against having the VP per turn from a new building or Lady starting on month one.

[b]Mid-Game[/b]: The 6/5's start with the initiative lead and never relinquish it. They begin to recruit key pieces like 1-Farmer and 1-Apothecary as early as possible because this has a musical chairs effect where the other players follow suit and somebody gets left out. This is important because low initiative players are already out of the initiative race and they need the per-piece efficiency of those elders. 6/5's get to build and food for free, while at the same time hopefully blocking the tax action. 6/5's can also recruit Lady if their lead is secure. Tax Collector is another good recruit because you can tax to fully survive Tribute, while possibly blocking a good action paired with tax, as opposed to staying off the board to refresh to 3. Any extra money may later become a small privilege.

The fundamental problem is that high initiative is so good when one or two people have it, and so bad when three or more compete for it - meaning no third person should adopt the strategy as it is a certain loss. Thus, the only way to compete with the 6/5's is by using pieces with very low initiative (Lady, Builder, Tax Collector) and then the 6/5's can do much the same thing - recruit efficient, low initiative pieces as long as the initiative lead is safe. If low initiative has a magical trump card I've missed, the 6/5's still retain the power to apply pressure, either by drafting certain key pieces first or taking certain actions first.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Feld classic, what a harsh game this is :). Not typical Feld that there are different ways to score points. Your just trying to keep your persons in your kingdom when bombarded with bad events. Very good game

RATING: 8
The game mechanic that decides the player turn order is rather innovative and creates a lot of tension and player interaction in the game. The other game mechanic involving planning for the bad events during the year introduces a lot of depth into the game. The best part of it all is, despite its depth, play time is still rather short.

Some of the scoring seems skewed. Privileges costs the same amount of yuan throughout the game, but its value decreases linearly over the twelve months. No characters actually affect the purchase of privileges, so privileges can probably be removed completely from the game. The scoring for Mongol Invasion also seems low compared to say the Festival, i.e. there is less incentive for a player to finish first for helmets than for fireworks, though there is incentive to not finish last for the helmets.

Lastly, the artwork is at best average.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A very punishing game that tests one's ability to plan ahead.  In the Year of the Dragon is composed of a total of 12 turns, in which 8 of them contain some sort of disasterous events that will either exhaust your resources or cost you to lose your subjects.

My initial impression is that unless you get very lucky in the arrangement of the event tiles, it is nearly impossible to escape unscathed as you go through the game.  However, it isn't as bad as it sounds, because most of the personnel you recruit will no longer serve much purpose after certain incidents have passed, so they are expendable in a sense, even though you do receive 2 VP for every one that you are able to keep until the end.

The biggest problem I have with the game is in the scoring, and how overpowered the privileges and the Court Ladies are because they count as points after every single round.  So for example, if you pick a Court Lady as one of your two starting person, then buy the large privilege (with 2 dragons) on your first turn, that means you will be gaining 5 points (including the 2 palaces you begin with) for a total of 60 for the entire game even if you never construct additional buildings and discard all other subjects.  That just seem completely unbalanced to me, the ability to score that high no matter how poorly you do for the rest of the game.

Still, this is one of the better titles to come out of Essen in 2007, and I'm looking forward to trying it again.

Update #1: I'm seriously beginning to dislike this game, a lot.  Every play through is the same, regardless how the event tiles come out, it always ends up as a contest of influence.  I have tried several strategies that basically ignore the influence track and although I do not lose badly, there is no way to win either.  Due to the fact that influence dictates when you get to select the action tiles, going last every round means you get whatever is left.  Unfortunately, these actions are not created nor balanced equally.  Taxes and build are useful no matter what the situation is and rarely do they get skipped.  Privilege is only good if you have the money to spend.  Fireworks display and harvest are only situationally good, sure you can plan ahead, but after a certain point they are completely worthless.  Research and military parade are crap, period, especially to someone that isn't competing in influence.

A game that only rewards one type of play is poorly designed, and I'm afraid that In the Year of the Dragon is beginning to look guilty of that more and more.  Oh, by the way, I take back what I said about the court lady.  It has got to be the most severely under powered character.  Only 1 influence for 1 point per turn?  What a joke.  I've lowered the score from its original 7.5 as a result, won't miss this if I never play it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After 5+ Plays: I've liked this one since I first tried it.  It has a nice mix of playing against a brutal game system, and vying with the other players who are trying to trip you up.  With further plays I have been somewhat disappointed to find only one effective strategy.  This doesn't ruin the game, as the tactical choices are still great fun, but it's not as good as it could have been. 

After 10+ Plays: I'm still enjoying this one a lot, though there are a couple of quirks that bug me.  The first is that whoever goes first gets a big boost from a first turn double privilege, assuming the tax event isn't very early.  The second is that it appears to be impossible to win without staying near the front of the turn order track.  Good players can simply plan for this and it doesn't break the game, but it would be more interesting if there was a way to at least occasionally win from the back of the turn order pack.

--The minor expansion is worth picking up.  The major events are a nice touch, and while I've only played with the wall cards once, they seem like an interesting twist.

RATING: 6
19/9/2016
Played it 9 times in the past 6 years. I'm not sure how proficient one can be at a game when the plays are so few and far between. The reason i'm saying this is because as a group we have an issue with regards to the large Privilege strategy on the first turn. 8 out of 9 times the game has been won by the person who implements that strategy. The one exception was when a newbie to the game with 3 other experienced players tried it and lost.

This is certainly a beginner strategy that seems too strong and when other players don't get enough plays to become good at the game this becomes an issue. Maybe that's a problem with a group that does not play the games enough times.

As for the game itself, i like it. I have not played anything similar to this. There is a fair amount of interaction in almost all phases of the game (which action is taken, specialists, rankings for the events and turn order). There is a constant struggle against the bad things that are happening to you and your people but i don't mind that at all

I enjoy the game but would have enjoyed it more without the large Privilege Strategy.
